What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Understanding Istifham Taqriri in Quranic Dialectic
Author defines istifham taqriri and provides Quranic examples of its use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and function of istifham taqriri in Quranic argument and distinguish it from Greek dialectical method.

Core Idea
Quranic interrogation uses undeniable self-evident premises, not mere rhetoric.

In Quranic debates, a unique form of questioning appears: istifham taqriri (interrogation for confirmation). This lesson unpacks its definition, contrasts it with the Greek dialectical method, and shows how it employs universally accepted premises to compel recognition of truth. You will learn to spot this tool in Quranic verses and appreciate its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its genus
Find the term 'istifham taqriri' in the Arabic. Note that it is a type of interrogation (istifham). The author defines it as questioning about self-evident demonstrative premises.
Genus: interrogation; differentia: about self-evident premises.
2
Understand the premise type
The premises used are 'al-muqaddimat al-bayyina al-burhaniyya' – clear, demonstrative premises that no one can deny. They are not merely accepted by the opponent but are universally evident.
This is a key contrast with Greek dialectic which relies on premises accepted by the opponent.
3
Contrast with Greek dialectical method
The Quran does not use premises just because the opponent accepts them; it uses premises that all people naturally accept, making the argument more compelling and binding.
The author explicitly distinguishes Quranic method from 'al-tariqa al-jadaliyya al-ma'rufa' among logicians.
4
Analyze the Quranic examples
Examine each verse: (1) 36:81 – God's power to resurrect; (2) 90:8-10 – blessings of eyes, tongue; (3) 52:35-43 – series of rhetorical questions exposing polytheism's absurdity. Note how each question leads to a self-evident truth.
These examples show the method in action: questions that force acknowledgment of obvious facts.
5
Recognize the argumentative function
Realize that istifham taqriri is not merely rhetorical; it is a logical tool that stimulates the soul, clarifies truth, and binds the addressee to admit falsehood's rejection.
The author emphasizes 'istithara wa bayan' (stimulation and clarification) to strengthen the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - الاستفهام التقريري: وهو الاستفهام عن المقدمات البيّنة البرهانية التي لا يمكن لأحد أن يجحدها، تدل على المطلوب لتقرير المخاطب الحق ولاعترافه بإنكار الباطل. والقرآن لا يستدل في مجادلاته بمقدمة لمجرد تسليم الخصم بها كما الطريقة الجدلية المعروفة عند أهل المنطق، بل يستدل بالقضايا والمقدمات التي يسلمها الناس لتكون أدعى للانقياد للحق ومجانبة الباطل، ولا شك أن في الاستفهام استثارة وبيانا لما في النفوس، ليكون الإلزام أبلغ وأقوى. ومن أمثلة الاستفهام التقريري في القرآن قوله تعالى: {أَوَلَيْسَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ بِقَادِرٍ عَلَى أَنْ يَخْلُقَ مِثْلَهُمْ بَلَى وَهُوَ الْخَلَّاقُ الْعَلِيمُ} وقوله تعالى: {أَلَمْ نَجْعَلْ لَهُ عَيْنَيْنِ وَلِسَانًا وَشَفَتَيْنِ وَهَدَيْنَاهُ النَّجْدَيْنِ} وقوله تعالى: {أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَالِقُونَ أَمْ خَلَقُوا السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ بَلْ لَا يُوقِنُونَ أَمْ عِنْدَهُمْ خَزَائِنُ رَبِّكَ أَمْ هُمُ الْمُصَيْطِرُونَ أَمْ لَهُمْ سُلَّمٌ يَسْتَمِعُونَ فِيهِ فَلْيَأْتِ مُسْتَمِعُهُمْ بِسُلْطَانٍ مُبِينٍ أَمْ لَهُ الْبَنَاتُ وَلَكُمُ الْبَنُونَ أَمْ تَسْأَلُهُمْ أَجْرًا فَهُمْ مِنْ مَغْرَمٍ مُثْقَلُونَ أَمْ عِنْدَهُمُ الْغَيْبُ فَهُمْ يَكْتُبُونَ أَمْ يُرِيدُونَ كَيْدًا فَالَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا هُمُ الْمَكِيدُونَ أَمْ لَهُمْ إِلَهٌ غَيْرُ اللَّهِ سُبْحَانَ اللَّهِ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ}
B - Al-Istifham al-Taqriri (Interrogation for confirmation): It is interrogation about self-evident demonstrative premises that no one can deny; it indicates the required conclusion to confirm the truth to the addressee and his admission of denying falsehood. And the Quran does not argue in its debates by relying on a premise merely because the opponent accepts it, as is the well-known dialectical method among the logicians. Rather, it argues by using propositions and premises that people accept, so that it is more conducive to submitting to truth and avoiding falsehood. There is no doubt that in interrogation there is stimulation and clarification of what is in the souls, so that the binding proof is more effective and stronger. Among examples of interrogation for confirmation in the Quran is His saying: 'Is not He who created the heavens and the earth able to create the like of them? Yes, and He is the All-Creator, the All-Knowing.' And His saying: 'Did We not make for him two eyes, a tongue, and two lips, and guide him to the two ways?' And His saying: 'Were they created from nothing, or are they the creators? Or did they create the heavens and the earth? Rather, they are not certain. Or are the treasures of your Lord with them, or are they the controllers? Or have they a ladder by which they listen? Then let their listener bring a clear authority. Or does He have daughters while you have sons? Or do you ask them for a reward so they are burdened with debt? Or do they have knowledge of the unseen, so they write it? Or do they intend a plot? Then those who disbelieve are the plotted against. Or have they a god other than Allah? Exalted is Allah above what they associate.'
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines al-istifham al-taqriri (interrogation for confirmation), a dialectical method used in the Quran. It is a question about self-evident, demonstrative premises that no one can deny, designed to affirm truth and refute falsehood. The author emphasizes that the Quran does not rely on premises accepted merely by the opponent (as in dialectic per logicians) but uses premises universally acknowledged, making the argument more compelling. Examples include: (1) Surah Yasin 36:81, questioning God’s power to resurrect; (2) Surah Al-Balad 90:8-10, reminding of the blessings of eyes, tongue, lips, and guidance; (3) Surah At-Tur 52:35-43, a series of rhetorical questions exposing the baselessness of polytheistic claims.

Key Terms
الاستفهام التقريري
المقدمات البيّنة البرهانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes Quranic interrogation from standard dialectical method?
A) It uses premises accepted only by the opponent
B) It uses self-evident premises that everyone accepts
C) It avoids interrogation and uses only declarative statements
D) It relies on analogy

Key Takeaway
Istifham taqriri is a uniquely compelling Quranic dialectical method based on undeniable truths, not mere dialectical acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a Quranic verse that uses istifham taqriri (other than those given). Explain how the question relies on a self-evident premise and how it functions to confirm truth.
?
Compare the Quranic method with the Greek dialectical method described by the author. Write a short paragraph showing why Quranic interrogation is more binding.

Abu Hanifa's Hierarchy of Sources
Abu Hanifa's methodological statement on source hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's four-level hierarchy of sources (Quran, Sunnah, Companions, ijtihad) to a new fiqh question.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's ijtihad follows a clear four-step hierarchy: Quran, then Sunnah, then Companions, then independent reasoning.

Imam Abu Hanifa famously articulated his method in a direct statement. This lesson unpacks that statement, revealing a structured priority of sources that guides all Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four levels
Read the quote and list the sources in order: Kitab Allah, Sunnah, sayings of the Companions, and finally ijtihad (reasoning) when no earlier text is found.
Note that 'Companions' includes multiple individuals, and ijtihad is only for matters not covered by texts.
2
Determine the condition for moving to the next level
The phrase 'if I do not find it therein' shows that each level is only used when the previous level is silent or inconclusive.
This conditional sequence ensures priority of revelation over human reasoning.
3
Apply the hierarchy to a hypothetical case
Given a new issue: if there is a clear Quranic verse, stop there. If no Quran but a Sahih hadith, use that. If neither, check Companion statements. If none of those, perform ijtihad.
This prevents arbitrary skipping of textual sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : تكون المذاهب الفقهية وتميز مناهجها الأصولية هذا وقد اتصل تاريخ هذا العهد - أي عهد أتباع التابعين - بتكون المذاهب الفقهية وتوضح مناهجها وقواعد أئمتها في استنباط الأحكام وتميزها بأئمتها وتلاميذها . ثم لا ثم فهذا الإمام أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه (80) - 150هـ) يبين مذهبه والقواعد التي يقيمه عليها بقوله : «إني آخذ بكتاب الله إذا وجدته، فما لم أجده فيه أخذت بسنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم والآثار الصحاح عنه التي فشت في أيدي الثقات، فإذا لم أجد في كتاب الله وسنة رسوله و أخذت بقول أصحابه؛ آخذ بقول من شئت، أعدل عن قولهم إلى قول غيرهم، فإذا انتهى الأمر إلى إبراهيم والشعبي (1) وابن المسيب وعدد رجالا فلي أن اجتهد كما اجتهدوا» . وهذه القواعد والأصول العامة التي يبني عليها الإمام أبو حنيفة مذهبه تنضاف إليها قواعد فرعية مفرعة على هذه الأصول أو راجعة إليها منها : (اللفظ العام قطعي في دلالته كالخاص مذهب الصحابي على خلاف العموم مخصص له)، كثرة رواة الحديث لا تفيد رجحانه)، (مفهوم الشرط والصفة لا اعتبار له)، (لا يقبل خبر الواحد فيما تعم به البلوى مقتضى الأمر الوجوب قطعا ما لم يرد صارف)، (يتقدم القياس الجلي على خبر الواحد المعارض له)، (الأخذ بالاستحسان وترك القياس عندما تظهر إلى ذلك حاجة). وهذه القواعد - في الحقيقة - لم تَصِحُ بها رواية صريحة عن الإمام ولا عن صاحبيه، وإنما أخذها علماء المذهب الحنفي بالاستقراء لفروع أئمتهم
The second section: The formation of the jurisprudential schools and the distinction of their methodological principles. And the history of this era - i.e., the era of the Followers of the Followers - was connected with the formation of the jurisprudential schools and the clarification of their methodologies and the principles of their imams in deriving rulings, and their distinction by their imams and students. Then, no, then this Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) (80-150 AH) explains his school and the principles upon which he establishes it by his saying: 'I take from the Book of Allah if I find it, and if I do not find it therein, I take from the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the authentic narrations from him that are widespread among reliable narrators. If I do not find in the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger, I take from the sayings of his Companions; I take from whomever I wish, and I deviate from their saying to the saying of others. When the matter reaches Ibrahim, al-Sha'bi, Ibn al-Musayyab, and a number of men, I have the right to exercise ijtihad as they did.' These are the general principles and fundamentals upon which Imam Abu Hanifa builds his school, to which are added subsidiary principles derived from or returning to these fundamentals, including: (The general word is definitive in its indication like the specific; the opinion of a Companion contrary to the general is a specification of it); (The multiplicity of narrators of a hadith does not give it preponderance); (The implication of condition and description is not valid); (The solitary report is not accepted in matters that are generally applicable; the imperative indicates obligation absolutely unless a diverting factor comes); (The clear analogy precedes the solitary report opposing it); (Resorting to istihsan and abandoning analogy when a need for that appears). And these principles - in reality - are not established by an explicit narration from the Imam nor from his two companions, but rather the scholars of the Hanafi school derived them by induction from the branches of their imams.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the methodological principles of Imam Abu Hanifa as understood by later Hanafi scholars. It opens by contextualizing the formation of madhhabs in the era of the Followers of the Followers. Then it quotes Abu Hanifa's own statement on his hierarchy of sources: first the Quran, then the Sunnah, then the sayings of the Companions, then independent ijtihad when the matter reaches certain early authorities. The passage then lists subsidiary principles that Hanafi scholars derived from his rulings: the general word is definitive; a Companion's opinion can specify a general text; multiplicity of narrators does not outweigh other factors; implications of condition and description are not given weight; solitary hadith are not accepted in widely applicable matters; clear analogy takes precedence over a solitary hadith; and istihsan (juristic preference) is used when analogical reasoning requires modification. The passage notes that these subsidiary principles were not explicitly narrated from Abu Hanifa but were extrapolated by later scholars via induction of his positive rulings. This is an example of how later usuli doctrine is reconstructed from the practice of an imam.

Key Terms
كتاب الله
سنة رسول الله
آثار الصحاح
أصحابه
اجتهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's statement in the passage, what does he do after the Quran and Sunnah have been exhausted?
A) He uses analogy directly
B) He takes from the sayings of the Companions
C) He stops and does not issue a ruling
D) He exercises ijtihad immediately

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's method prioritizes revelation at every step, allowing reasoning only after all textual sources are exhausted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine a new issue arises about the permissibility of genetically modified food. No Quranic verse, no authentic hadith, and no Companion statement directly addresses it. According to Abu Hanifa's method, what should the jurist do? Construct the appropriate step.

What Is Mantiq?

The Hanafi Principle: The General Word Is Definitive
Subsidiary principle on general words derived from Abu Hanifa's rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a Hanafi jurist treats a general word as definitive and when a Companion's opinion can specify it.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, a general word (al-lafz al-‘amm) gives definitive meaning, and only a Companion's explicit contrary opinion can restrict it.

One of the subsidiary principles derived from Abu Hanifa's rulings is that general words in revelation are not ambiguous but carry certain implication. This lesson explores that principle and its one exception: a Companion's opinion can serve as a specifier.

Guided Path
1
Define the general word (al-lafz al-‘amm)
A word that encompasses multiple individuals or types without restriction, e.g., 'المسلمون' (the Muslims).
In Arabic usul, 'amm is a category of lafz.
2
Understand the claim of definitiveness
The passage states that al-lafz al-‘amm is 'qat'i fi dalalatihi' – its indication is definitive, like that of a specific word. This means it is not open to interpretation or ta'wil without strong evidence.
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'is) consider 'amm as zanni (probable) and allow specification by various indicators.
3
Identify the exception: Companion's opinion
If a Companion holds a view that contradicts the apparent generality, that opinion can serve as a mukhassis (specifier), limiting the general word.
This is a unique Hanafi position; other schools may require a proof from Quran or Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : تكون المذاهب الفقهية وتميز مناهجها الأصولية هذا وقد اتصل تاريخ هذا العهد - أي عهد أتباع التابعين - بتكون المذاهب الفقهية وتوضح مناهجها وقواعد أئمتها في استنباط الأحكام وتميزها بأئمتها وتلاميذها . ثم لا ثم فهذا الإمام أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه (80) - 150هـ) يبين مذهبه والقواعد التي يقيمه عليها بقوله : «إني آخذ بكتاب الله إذا وجدته، فما لم أجده فيه أخذت بسنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم والآثار الصحاح عنه التي فشت في أيدي الثقات، فإذا لم أجد في كتاب الله وسنة رسوله و أخذت بقول أصحابه؛ آخذ بقول من شئت، أعدل عن قولهم إلى قول غيرهم، فإذا انتهى الأمر إلى إبراهيم والشعبي (1) وابن المسيب وعدد رجالا فلي أن اجتهد كما اجتهدوا» . وهذه القواعد والأصول العامة التي يبني عليها الإمام أبو حنيفة مذهبه تنضاف إليها قواعد فرعية مفرعة على هذه الأصول أو راجعة إليها منها : (اللفظ العام قطعي في دلالته كالخاص مذهب الصحابي على خلاف العموم مخصص له)، كثرة رواة الحديث لا تفيد رجحانه)، (مفهوم الشرط والصفة لا اعتبار له)، (لا يقبل خبر الواحد فيما تعم به البلوى مقتضى الأمر الوجوب قطعا ما لم يرد صارف)، (يتقدم القياس الجلي على خبر الواحد المعارض له)، (الأخذ بالاستحسان وترك القياس عندما تظهر إلى ذلك حاجة). وهذه القواعد - في الحقيقة - لم تَصِحُ بها رواية صريحة عن الإمام ولا عن صاحبيه، وإنما أخذها علماء المذهب الحنفي بالاستقراء لفروع أئمتهم
The second section: The formation of the jurisprudential schools and the distinction of their methodological principles. And the history of this era - i.e., the era of the Followers of the Followers - was connected with the formation of the jurisprudential schools and the clarification of their methodologies and the principles of their imams in deriving rulings, and their distinction by their imams and students. Then, no, then this Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) (80-150 AH) explains his school and the principles upon which he establishes it by his saying: 'I take from the Book of Allah if I find it, and if I do not find it therein, I take from the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the authentic narrations from him that are widespread among reliable narrators. If I do not find in the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger, I take from the sayings of his Companions; I take from whomever I wish, and I deviate from their saying to the saying of others. When the matter reaches Ibrahim, al-Sha'bi, Ibn al-Musayyab, and a number of men, I have the right to exercise ijtihad as they did.' These are the general principles and fundamentals upon which Imam Abu Hanifa builds his school, to which are added subsidiary principles derived from or returning to these fundamentals, including: (The general word is definitive in its indication like the specific; the opinion of a Companion contrary to the general is a specification of it); (The multiplicity of narrators of a hadith does not give it preponderance); (The implication of condition and description is not valid); (The solitary report is not accepted in matters that are generally applicable; the imperative indicates obligation absolutely unless a diverting factor comes); (The clear analogy precedes the solitary report opposing it); (Resorting to istihsan and abandoning analogy when a need for that appears). According to the Hanafi principle in the passage, what status does the general word (al-lafz al-‘amm) have?

?

Hanafi Rule: Clear Analogy Precedes Solitary Hadith
Subsidiary principle on the precedence of clear analogy over solitary hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a clear analogy (qiyas jali) takes precedence over a solitary hadith when they conflict.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, a clear analogy is stronger than a solitary hadith when both address the same issue.

Another subsidiary principle derived from Abu Hanifa's practice is that if a clear analogical reasoning conflicts with a solitary hadith (khabar al-wahid), the analogy is preferred. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this prioritization.

Guided Path
1
Define clear analogy (qiyas jali)
An analogical deduction where the effective cause ('illah) is clearly indicated in the original text, leaving little room for doubt.
Example: prohibition of wine due to intoxication is extended to all intoxicants.
2
Define solitary hadith (khabar al-wahid)
A hadith transmitted by a single narrator or a small chain that does not reach the level of tawatur (mass transmission). Its authenticity is probable but not certain.
Hanafis categorize khabar al-wahid as zanni (probable).
3
Understand the conflict and rule
When a solitary hadith contradicts a clear analogy, the Hanafi principle gives precedence to the clear analogy because the analogy is based on a clear textual indication, whereas the solitary hadith is only probable.
This rule applies especially to matters of general applicability (umum al-balwa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : تكون المذاهب الفقهية وتميز مناهجها الأصولية هذا وقد اتصل تاريخ هذا العهد - أي عهد أتباع التابعين - بتكون المذاهب الفقهية وتوضح مناهجها وقواعد أئمتها في استنباط الأحكام وتميزها بأئمتها وتلاميذها . ثم لا ثم فهذا الإمام أبو حنيفة رضي الله عنه (80) - 150هـ) يبين مذهبه والقواعد التي يقيمه عليها بقوله : «إني آخذ بكتاب الله إذا وجدته، فما لم أجده فيه أخذت بسنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم والآثار الصحاح عنه التي فشت في أيدي الثقات، فإذا لم أجد في كتاب الله وسنة رسوله و أخذت بقول أصحابه؛ آخذ بقول من شئت، أعدل عن قولهم إلى قول غيرهم، فإذا انتهى الأمر إلى إبراهيم والشعبي (1) وابن المسيب وعدد رجالا فلي أن اجتهد كما اجتهدوا» . وهذه القواعد والأصول العامة التي يبني عليها الإمام أبو حنيفة مذهبه تنضاف إليها قواعد فرعية مفرعة على هذه الأصول أو راجعة إليها منها : (اللفظ العام قطعي في دلالته كالخاص مذهب الصحابي على خلاف العموم مخصص له)، كثرة رواة الحديث لا تفيد رجحانه)، (مفهوم الشرط والصفة لا اعتبار له)، (لا يقبل خبر الواحد فيما تعم به البلوى مقتضى الأمر الوجوب قطعا ما لم يرد صارف)، (يتقدم القياس الجلي على خبر الواحد المعارض له)، (الأخذ بالاستحسان وترك القياس عندما تظهر إلى ذلك حاجة). وهذه القواعد - في الحقيقة - لم تَصِحُ بها رواية صريحة عن الإمام ولا عن صاحبيه، وإنما أخذها علماء المذهب الحنفي بالاستقراء لفروع أئمتهم
The second section: The formation of the jurisprudential schools and the distinction of their methodological principles. And the history of this era - i.e., the era of the Followers of the Followers - was connected with the formation of the jurisprudential schools and the clarification of their methodologies and the principles of their imams in deriving rulings, and their distinction by their imams and students. Then, no, then this Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) (80-150 AH) explains his school and the principles upon which he establishes it by his saying: 'I take from the Book of Allah if I find it, and if I do not find it therein, I take from the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and the authentic narrations from him that are widespread among reliable narrators. If I do not find in the Book of Allah and the Sunnah of His Messenger, I take from the sayings of his Companions; I take from whomever I wish, and I deviate from their saying to the saying of others. When the matter reaches Ibrahim, al-Sha'bi, Ibn al-Musayyab, and a number of men, I have the right to exercise ijtihad as they did.' These are the general principles and fundamentals upon which Imam Abu Hanifa builds his school, to which are added subsidiary principles derived from or returning to these fundamentals, including: (The general word is definitive in its indication like the specific; the opinion of a Companion contrary to the general is a specification of it); (The multiplicity of narrators of a hadith does not give it preponderance); (The implication of condition and description is not valid); (The solitary report is not accepted in matters that are generally applicable; the imperative indicates obligation absolutely unless a diverting factor comes); (The clear analogy precedes the solitary report opposing it); (Resorting to istihsan and abandoning analogy when a need for that appears). And these principles - in reality - are not established by an explicit narration from the Imam nor from his two companions, but rather the scholars of the Hanafi school derived them by induction from the branches of their imams.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the methodological principles of Imam Abu Hanifa as understood by later Hanafi scholars. It opens by contextualizing the formation of madhhabs in the era of the Followers of the Followers. Then it quotes Abu Hanifa's own statement on his hierarchy of sources: first the Quran, then the Sunnah, then the sayings of the Companions, then independent ijtihad when the matter reaches certain early authorities. The passage then lists subsidiary principles that Hanafi scholars derived from his rulings: the general word is definitive; a Companion's opinion can specify a general text; multiplicity of narrators does not outweigh other factors; implications of condition and description are not given weight; solitary hadith are not accepted in widely applicable matters; clear analogy takes precedence over a solitary hadith; and istihsan (juristic preference) is used when analogical reasoning requires modification. The passage notes that these subsidiary principles were not explicitly narrated from Abu Hanifa but were extrapolated by later scholars via induction of his positive rulings. This is an example of how later usuli doctrine is reconstructed from the practice of an imam.

خبر الواحد
معارض

In Hanafi usul, clear analogy outweighs solitary hadith when they are in conflict.

Imam Malik's Methodological Principles in Usul al-Fiqh
Author enumerates Imam Malik's usuli principles derived from his school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the primary and secondary sources of Maliki usul al-fiqh as enumerated by the author, identifying which are unique to the Maliki school.

Core Idea
Imam Malik's usul al-fiqh is a unique synthesis of textual and customary sources, including 'amal ahl al-Madinah and sadd al-dhara'i'.

In this passage, the author outlines the methodological principles of Imam Malik's school, showing how Malik's approach differs from other madhabs. The list begins with the primary textual sources from the Quran and then moves to supplementary sources like consensus, analogy, and distinctive Maliki principles. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for appreciating how Maliki jurists derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first five principles from the Quran
The author lists five principles derived from the Quranic text: the clear text (nass), its apparent meaning (zahir), its inferred meaning (dalil = mafhum al-mukhalafa), its explicit concept (mafhum al-muwafaqa), and its indication of the cause (tanbih). Note how each level builds on the previous.
These five, when doubled by considering their opposites, become ten.
2
Understand the concept of mafhum and tanbih
Mafhum al-mukhalafa (concept of opposition) implies that if a condition is stated, its absence yields the opposite ruling. Mafhum al-muwafaqa (concept of agreement) means that if a ruling is stated for something, a similar thing with a stronger reason also gets the same ruling. Tanbih is an indication of the 'illah (cause), e.g., the Quran calling something 'abomination' indicates its prohibition.
The example given is from the Quran: 'For it is an abomination or sin', which implies prohibition.
3
Recognize the sources after the textual ones
After the ten textual principles, the author lists: ijma' (consensus), qiyas (analogy), 'amal ahl al-Madinah (practice of the people of Madinah), istihsan (juristic preference), sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil), masalih mursala (public interests), qawl al-sahabi (saying of a Companion), ri'ayat al-khilaf (consideration of valid disagreement), istishab (presumption of continuity), and shar' man qablana (laws of previous prophets).
This order indicates the hierarchy of evidence in Maliki usul.
4
Identify the distinctive Maliki sources
Two sources stand out as unique to Malik's school: 'amal ahl al-Madinah (the continuous practice of Medina's people) and sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil). While other schools may use similar concepts, Malik elevates them to formal principles.
For example, 'amal ahl al-Madinah is not accepted by Shafi'i or Hanbali schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الإمام مالك حمد لله (93) - 179هـ) الذي يتصل مذهبه بمذهب الحجازيين أصحاب مدرسة الإمام سعيد بن المسيب، فذو منهج مختلف، وتتلخص قواعد مذهبه فيما يلي : الأخذ بنص الكتاب العزيز، ثم بظاهره وهو العموم، ثم بدليله وهو مفهوم المخالفة، ثم بمفهومه (وهو) مفهوم الموافقة، ثم بتنبيهه : وهو التنبيه على العلة، كقوله تعالى : ﴿فَإِنةَ رِجْسٌ أَوْ فِسْقا، وهذه أصول خمسة ومن مثلها، فتكون عشرة، ثم الإجماع، ثم القياس، ثم عمل أهل المدينة، ثم الاستحسان، ثم الحكم بسد الذرائع ، ثم المصالح المرسلة، ثم قول الصحابي إن سنده وكان من الأعلام، ثم مراعاة الخلاف إذا قوي دليل المخالف، ثم الاستصحاب، ثم شرع من قبلنا
As for Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) (93-179 AH), whose school is connected with the school of the Hijazis, the followers of the school of Imam Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, he has a different methodology. And the principles of his school are summarized as follows: taking from the clear text of the Mighty Book, then its apparent meaning which is the general, then its inferred meaning which is the concept of opposition (mukhālafa), then its explicit concept which is the concept of agreement (muwāfaqa), then its indication which is the indication of the cause (tanbīh), like His saying exalted: 'For it is an abomination or sin' (Qur'an). These are five principles and from their likes, they become ten. Then consensus (ijmā'), then analogy (qiyās), then the practice of the people of Madinah (ʿamal ahl al-Madīna), then juristic preference (istiḥsān), then the principle of blocking the means (sadd al-dharāʾiʿ), then considerations of public interest (al-maṣāliḥ al-mursala), then the saying of a Companion if his chain is authentic and he was among the prominent scholars, then taking into account disagreement (khilāf) if the evidence of the opponent is strong, then presumption of continuity (istiṣḥāb), then the law of those before us (sharʿ man qablanā).
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the methodological principles of Imam Malik's school as derived by later Malikis. It begins by noting Malik's connection to the Hijazi school of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab. Then it lists the sources in order: first the clear text of the Quran, then its apparent meaning (general), then its dalīl which is the concept of opposition (mufhūm al-mukhālafa), then the explicit concept (mufhūm al-muwāfaqa), then the tanbīh (an indication of the cause, e.g., the verse calling something 'abomination or sin' indicates prohibition). These five are considered primary, and by considering their opposites they become ten. After these textual sources come: consensus, analogy, the practice of the people of Madinah, istihsan, sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil), masalih mursala (unregulated public interests), the saying of a Companion (if reliable), consideration of valid disagreement, istishab (presumption of continuity), and laws of previous prophets. This enumeration shows the scope of Malik's usul, which includes elements not found in other schools, such as 'amal ahl al-Madinah and a specific view on mufhūm. The passage is purely descriptive and presents the principles as a list without further justification or critique.

عمل أهل المدينة

Imam Malik's usul al-fiqh includes both common sources like Quran and consensus, and unique principles like 'amal ahl al-Madinah and sadd al-dhara'i', making his methodology distinct from other schools.

Imam al-Shafi'i's Methodological Principles and Rejections
Author presents al-Shafi'i's usuli principles and his rejections of other schools' methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate al-Shafi'i's core usuli principles and identify which sources he rejected.

Core Idea
Al-Shafi'i anchors fiqh on Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas with a clear cause, rejecting istihsan, masalih mursala, and 'amal ahl al-Madinah.

Imam al-Shafi'i established a rigorous methodological framework for Islamic jurisprudence, emphasizing textual sources and strict conditions for analogy. This lesson walks through his foundational principles as he himself articulated them, and then examines his well-known rejections of other schools' methods. Understanding these principles is essential for grasping the Shafi'i school's approach to legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational sources
Al-Shafi'i begins by stating that the Quran and Sunnah are the primary sources. If they do not provide a ruling, then analogy (qiyas) upon them is used. This establishes a clear hierarchy.
2
Parse conditions for hadith acceptance
He gives rules for hadith: authentic chain, apparent meaning, preference for closest meaning if multiple, and choosing the most authentic chain among conflicting hadith.
Note the exception for mursal: only Ibn al-Musayyib's is accepted.
3
Examine limits of qiyas
Al-Shafi'i prohibits analogizing from one original to another (usul to usul) and restricts questions of 'why' to the branch (far'), not the original. Analogy must be based on a clear and precise cause (illa).
This reflects his strict view on qiyas.
4
List the rejected sources
After the quote, the passage details al-Shafi'i's rejections: istihsan, masalih mursala, analogy without a clear cause, 'amal ahl al-Madinah, and Hanafi abandonment of sunan due to extra conditions like shuhra.
These rejections define his school's boundary against other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قواعد وأصول مذهب الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله فهي ما أجمله في قوله : الأصل قرآن وسنة فإن لم يكن فقياس عليهما، وإذا اتصل الحديث عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وصح الإسناد به فهو المنتهى، والإجماع أكبر من الخبر المفرد، والحديث على ظاهره، وإذا احتمل المعاني فما أشبه منها ظاهره أولاها به، وإذا تكافأت الأحاديث فأصحها إسنادًا أولاها ، وليس المنقطع بشيء ماعدا منقطع ابن المسيب، ولا يقاس أصل على أصل ولا يقال لأصل لم وكيف؟ وإنما يقال للفرع لم؟ فإذا صح قياسه على الأصل صح وقامت به الحجة»(3) وإضافة إلى ذلك فقد أنكر الإمام الشافعي الاحتجاج بالاستحسان، مخالفا في ذلك الحنفية والمالكية ،معا وكتب في رد الاستحسان كتابه (إبطال الاستحسان)، رد المصالح المرسلة وأنكر ،حجيتها وأنكر الاحتجاج بقياس لا يقوم على علة ظاهرة منضبطة، وأنكر الاحتجاج بعمل أهل المدينة، كما أنكر على الحنفية تركهم العمل بكثير من السنن لعدم توفر ما وضعوه فيها من الشروط كالشهرة ونحوها (4).
As for the principles and fundamentals of the school of Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him), they are what he summarized in his saying: 'The foundation is the Quran and the Sunnah, and if not, then analogy upon them. When a hadith is transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and its chain is authentic, it is the ultimate authority. Consensus is greater than the solitary report. The hadith is upon its apparent meaning. If it can bear multiple meanings, then what resembles its apparent meaning is most appropriate. If hadiths are equal in strength, then the most authentic in chain is preferred. The disconnected (mursal) is not anything except the disconnected of Ibn al-Musayyib. An original ruling is not measured by another original, and one does not say 'why' or 'how' about an original? One only says 'why' about the branch. If its analogy to the original is correct, then it is correct and the proof is established.' In addition to that, Imam al-Shafi'i rejected argumentation by istihsan, opposing in that the Hanafis and Malikis together. He wrote his book 'Ibtal al-Istihsan' in refutation of istihsan. He rejected the masalih mursala and denied its validity. He rejected argumentation by analogy that is not based on a clear and precise cause. He rejected argumentation by 'amal ahl al-Madinah. He also criticized the Hanafis for abandoning many sunan due to lack of conditions they had set like widespreadness (shuhra) and the like.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the methodological principles of Imam al-Shafi'i as expressed in his own words and then notes his rejections of other schools' sources. It begins by quoting al-Shafi'i's own summary: the foundation is the Quran and Sunnah, then analogy on them; authentic hadith are the final authority; consensus outweighs solitary hadith; hadith are interpreted according to their apparent meaning unless there is evidence for a different interpretation; when there are multiple possible meanings, the one closest to the apparent is preferred; among competing hadiths, the most authentic chain is given priority; mursal hadith (disconnected chain) is not accepted except from Ibn al-Musayyib; one does not analogize from an original to another original—the 'why' is asked about the branch, not the original; if the analogy to the original is sound, the proof is established. After the quote, the passage explains further that al-Shafi'i rejected istihsan (juristic preference), wrote a book against it, rejected masalih mursala (unrestricted public interest), rejected analogy without a clear cause, rejected 'amal ahl al-Madinah (practice of Madinans), and criticized Hanafis for setting conditions like shuhra (widespreadness) that led them to abandon many authentic hadiths. This demonstrates al-Shafi'i's strict adherence to textual sources and his critique of other schools' methodological expansions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's usul restricts legal reasoning to clear textual sources and analogy with an explicit cause, excluding istihsan, masalih mursala, and 'amal ahl al-Madinah.

Shafi'i's Hierarchy of Sources
Author presents Shafi'i's own summary of his principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hierarchy of sources in Shafi'i's usul al-fiqh and apply his rules for preferring hadith.

Core Idea
Quran and Sunnah are primary; qiyas is secondary; ijma' outweighs isolated hadith.

Imam al-Shafi'i laid down a clear set of principles for deriving legal rulings. In this lesson, we unpack his own summary, focusing on the sources he accepts and the order of priority he assigns them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary sources
Locate the sources Shafi'i lists as foundational: the Quran, the Sunnah, and qiyas (analogy).
Shafi'i explicitly says 'al-aṣl Quran wa-Sunnah'
2
Understand the hierarchy
Note the order: first the Quran and Sunnah, then qiyas. Also, ijma' is said to be greater than the isolated hadith (khabar al-wāḥid).
This establishes a priority when sources conflict.
3
Apply hadith preference rules
Shafi'i gives rules for contradictory hadith: prefer the one with the strongest chain, and interpret according to the apparent meaning (ẓāhir). Interrupted (munqaṭi‘) hadith is generally rejected except from Ibn al-Musayyib.
The apparent meaning is the default.
4
Distinguish original and branch
Shafi'i prohibits analogizing an original to another original; only a branch (far‘) can be compared to an original. One does not ask 'why' about the original, only about the branch.
This limits the scope of qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قواعد وأصول مذهب الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله فهي ما أجمله في : «الأصل قرآن وسنة فإن لم يكن فقياس عليهما، وإذا اتصل الحديث عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وصح الإسناد به فهو المنتهى، والإجماع أكبر من الخبر المفرد، والحديث على ظاهره، وإذا احتمل المعاني فما أشبه منها ظاهره أولاها به، وإذا تكافأت الأحاديث فأصحها إسنادا أولاها ، وليس المنقطع بشيء ماعدا منقطع ابن المسيب، ولا يقاس أصل على أصل ولا يقال لأصل لم وكيف؟ وإنما يقال للفرع لم؟ فإذا صح قياسه على الأصل صح وقامت به الحجة» وإضافة إلى ذلك فقد أنكر الإمام الشافعي الاحتجاج بالاستحسان، مخالفا في ذلك الحنفية والمالكية معا وكتب في رد الاستحسان كتابه (إبطال الاستحسان)، ورد المصالح المرسلة وأنكر حجيتها وأنكر الاحتجاج بقياس لا يقوم على علة ظاهرة منضبطة، وأنكر الاحتجاج بعمل أهل المدينة، كما أنكر على الحنفية تركهم العمل بكثير من السنن لعدم توفر ما وضعوه فيها من الشروط كالشهرة ونحوها.
As for the rules and principles of the school of Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, they are what he summarized in: 'The foundation is the Quran and Sunnah; if not, then analogy upon them. When a hadith from the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) is connected and its chain is authentic, that is the ultimate. Ijma' is greater than the isolated hadith. The hadith is to be taken according to its apparent meaning. If it admits multiple meanings, then what is closest to its apparent meaning is most appropriate. When hadiths are equivalent, the one with the strongest chain is most appropriate. The interrupted (munqati') is nothing, except the interrupted of Ibn al-Musayyab. An original case is not to be compared to another original case, nor is one to ask 'why?' about an original case. One only asks 'why?' about a branch case. When its analogy upon the original is valid, it is correct and the proof stands.' In addition to that, Imam al-Shafi'i rejected arguing by Istihsan, opposing the Hanafis and Malikis together, and wrote his book 'Ibtal al-Istihsan' on refuting Istihsan. He rejected the public interests (masalih mursala) and denied its authority, and denied arguing by analogy that is not based on a clear and precise cause. He also rejected arguing by the practice of the people of Medina, and he criticized the Hanafis for abandoning many hadiths because they lacked the conditions they set, such as notoriety (shuhra) and the like.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the core principles of Imam al-Shafi'i's usul al-fiqh. Shafi'i begins with the primacy of the Quran and Sunnah; if a ruling is not found there, analogy (qiyas) is used, but only based on the two primary sources. Authentic connected hadith (muttaṣil) is the ultimate authority, even over Ijma' which is greater than isolated (khabar al-wāḥid) hadith. He insists on the apparent meaning (ẓāhir) of hadith. When hadiths conflict, the one with the strongest chain is preferred. The interrupted chain (munqati‘) is generally weak, except for the famous one from Ibn al-Musayyib. He prohibits analogy between two original (aṣl) cases—only a branch (far‘) can be analogized to an original. One does not question the wisdom of the original ruling, only the branch. After quoting Shafi'i's own summary, the author notes Shafi'i's rejections: he opposed Istihsan (juristic preference), writing a book to refute it; he denied the authority of unregulated public interests (masāliḥ mursala); he rejected any analogy not founded on a clear cause; he dismissed the practice of Medina as a source; and he criticized the Hanafis for discarding many hadiths due to extra conditions like notoriety (shuhra). These points show Shafi'i's strict textualism.

الفرع
القياس

الحديث المتصل

Shafi'i's Rejections of Alternative Sources
Author enumerates Shafi'i's rejections of certain legal sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the sources of law rejected by Shafi'i and explain his reasoning for each rejection.

Core Idea
Shafi'i rejected Istihsan, masalih mursala, and practice of Medina as independent sources, demanding strict textual basis.

Beyond outlining his own methodology, Imam al-Shafi'i firmly rejected several principles used by other schools. In this lesson, we examine his objections to Istihsan, masalih mursala, analogy without a clear cause, and the practice of Medina.

Guided Path
1
List the rejected sources
Identify the four sources Shafi'i explicitly rejects: Istihsan (juristic preference), masalih mursala (unregulated public interests), qiyas without a clear cause, and 'amal ahl al-Madina (practice of Medina).
Also note his criticism of Hanafi abandonment of hadith.
2
Understand Shafi'i's objections
For each rejected source, find Shafi'i's stated reason: Istihsan is personal preference; masalih mursala lack textual basis; qiyas without a cause is arbitrary; Medina's practice is not universally binding.
He wrote a book refuting Istihsan.
3
Identify the required conditions for qiyas
Shafi'i demands a clear and precise cause ('illa ẓāhira munḍabiṭa) for analogy to be valid. This limits qiyas to cases where the rationale is evident from the texts.
This contrasts with Hanafi practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قواعد وأصول مذهب الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله فهي ما أجمله في : «الأصل قرآن وسنة فإن لم يكن فقياس عليهما، وإذا اتصل الحديث عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وصح الإسناد به فهو المنتهى، والإجماع أكبر من الخبر المفرد، والحديث على ظاهره، وإذا احتمل المعاني فما أشبه منها ظاهره أولاها به، وإذا تكافأت الأحاديث فأصحها إسنادا أولاها ، وليس المنقطع بشيء ماعدا منقطع ابن المسيب، ولا يقاس أصل على أصل ولا يقال لأصل لم وكيف؟ وإنما يقال للفرع لم؟ فإذا صح قياسه على الأصل صح وقامت به الحجة» وإضافة إلى ذلك فقد أنكر الإمام الشافعي الاحتجاج بالاستحسان، مخالفا في ذلك الحنفية والمالكية معا وكتب في رد الاستحسان كتابه (إبطال الاستحسان)، ورد المصالح المرسلة وأنكر حجيتها وأنكر الاحتجاج بقياس لا يقوم على علة ظاهرة منضبطة، وأنكر الاحتجاج بعمل أهل المدينة، كما أنكر على الحنفية تركهم العمل بكثير من السنن لعدم توفر ما وضعوه فيها من الشروط كالشهرة ونحوها.
As for the rules and principles of the school of Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, they are what he summarized in: 'The foundation is the Quran and Sunnah; if not, then analogy upon them. When a hadith from the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) is connected and its chain is authentic, that is the ultimate. Ijma' is greater than the isolated hadith. The hadith is to be taken according to its apparent meaning. If it admits multiple meanings, then what is closest to its apparent meaning is most appropriate. When hadiths are equivalent, the one with the strongest chain is most appropriate. The interrupted (munqati') is nothing, except the interrupted of Ibn al-Musayyab. An original case is not to be compared to another original case, nor is one to ask 'why?' about an original case. One only asks 'why?' about a branch case. When its analogy upon the original is valid, it is correct and the proof stands.' In addition to that, Imam al-Shafi'i rejected arguing by Istihsan, opposing the Hanafis and Malikis together, and wrote his book 'Ibtal al-Istihsan' on refuting Istihsan. He rejected the public interests (masalih mursala) and denied its authority, and denied arguing by analogy that is not based on a clear and precise cause. He also rejected arguing by the practice of the people of Medina, and he criticized the Hanafis for abandoning many hadiths because they lacked the conditions they set, such as notoriety (shuhra) and the like.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the core principles of Imam al-Shafi'i's usul al-fiqh. Shafi'i begins with the primacy of the Quran and Sunnah; if a ruling is not found there, analogy (qiyas) is used, but only based on the two primary sources. Authentic connected hadith (muttaṣil) is the ultimate authority, even over Ijma' which is greater than isolated (khabar al-wāḥid) hadith. He insists on the apparent meaning (ẓāhir) of hadith. When hadiths conflict, the one with the strongest chain is preferred. The interrupted chain (munqati‘) is generally weak, except for the famous one from Ibn al-Musayyib. He prohibits analogy between two original (aṣl) cases—only a branch (far‘) can be analogized to an original. One does not question the wisdom of the original ruling, only the branch. After quoting Shafi'i's own summary, the author notes Shafi'i's rejections: he opposed Istihsan (juristic preference), writing a book to refute it; he denied the authority of unregulated public interests (masāliḥ mursala); he rejected any analogy not founded on a clear cause; he dismissed the practice of Medina as a source; and he criticized the Hanafis for discarding many hadiths due to extra conditions like notoriety (shuhra). These points show Shafi'i's strict textualism.

Key Terms
الاستحسان
المصالح المرسلة
علة ظاهرة منضبطة
عمل أهل المدينة

Key Takeaway
Shafi'i strictly limited legal sources to texts and analogies derived from them, rejecting extra-textual methods like Istihsan and masalih mursala.

The Concrete Roots of Jadal: From Twisting Rope to Firm Argument
Author enumerates linguistic derivations of jadal from classical Arabic lexicons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core concrete meaning of the root ج د ل and explain how it informs the logical term 'jadal'.

Core Idea
Jadal begins as twisting rope; it ends as strong argument.

Before diving into the rules of dialectical debate (jadal), it is crucial to understand the root from which the term springs. Classical Arabic lexicons show that the root (ج د ل) revolves around the idea of twisting, joining, and firmness. This lesson explores eight concrete derivations, revealing how a physical concept becomes the foundation for rigorous intellectual exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core meaning
Read the opening definition: 'الجدل: وفتلته فتلا محكما' (argumentation: its twisting is a firm twisting). What is the fundamental action associated with the root? It is the idea of twisting something firmly.
The root does not simply mean 'twist' but 'twist firmly and skillfully'.
2
Trace the concrete applications
List each derived word (جديل، جدول، مجدول، جادل، جدول، مجدولة، أجدل، جدالة) and note how each object embodies firmness or twisting. For example, the small river (جدول) is called so because its water is strong and cohesive; the armor (مجدولة) is firm in its rings.
Pay attention to the physical objects: rope, river, man's build, animal, limbs, armor, falcon, ground.
3
Observe the transition to human and animal
Notice how the words apply to humans (مجدول for a slender but firm man, جادل for a strong boy) and animals (جادل for a strong camel calf, أجدل for a powerful falcon). This extends the concept from inanimate objects to living beings.
The core meaning of strength and firmness is preserved across all applications.
4
Connect to the abstract logical term
Reflect on how these concrete meanings might lead to the abstract term 'jadal' for dialectical argument. Arguments are 'twisted' together from premises and conclusions, and they must be 'firmly' constructed to be valid. The term thus carries connotations of craftsmanship and resilience.
This lexical foundation shows that jadal in logic is not mere quarreling but a structured, robust activity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل
وفتلته فتلا محكما، ومنه قيل لزمام الناقة، الجديل .. وهو الحبل المفتول من أدم أو شعر يكون في عنق البعير أو الناقة، والجمع جدل، كما يقال – أيضًا للفحل من الأنعام جديل.
2 - الجدول : وهو النهر الصغير، وهو ممتد وماؤه أقوى في اجتماع أجزائه من المنبطح السائح ، جمعه جداول .
3 - المَجْدُول : وهو الرجل إذا كان قَضِيفَ الخلقة من غير هزال، وبعبارة أخرى : اللطيف القصب المحكم، الفتل وأصل المعنى في هذه المادة : الفتل وضم شيء إلى آخر وإحكامه.
4 - الجَادِل : وهو الغلام إذا اشتد ، والجادل من ولد الناقة : هو الذي قوي ومشى مع أمه.
5 - الجُدول : وهي الأعضاء، واحدها جدل، وجدول الإنسان : قصب اليدين والرجلين ، يقال : ساق مجدولة وجدلاء ؛ حسنة الطي، وساعد أجدل كذلك قال الشاعر : فأخرجهم أجدل الساعدين أصهب كالأسد الأغلب
6 - المجدولة : وهي الدرع المحكمة العمل، المتينة الصناعة قال الأزهري : سميت الدروع جدلاء ومجدلة، لإحكام حلقها، كما يقال : حبل مجدول، مفتول وقد جدلت جدلا أي أحكمت إحكاما.
7 - الأجدل : الصقر، وسمي بذلك لأنه من أشد الطيور قوة قال الشاعر : كأن فراخ القطا لاقين أجدل بازيا
8 - الجدالة : وهي الأرض المطمئنة الصلبة، المحكمة وجهها ولذلك يقال : جدله جدلا وجدله فانجدل وتجدل ؛ صرعه على الجدالة، وهو مجدول ، وقد جدلته جدلا ، وأكثر ما يقال : جدلته تجديلًا، وقيل للصريع مجدل، لأنه يصرع على الجدالة قال الراجز :
Argumentation: And its twisting is a firm twisting, and from that was said for the nose-ring of the she-camel, 'al-jadīl'.. and it is the rope twisted from leather or hair that is on the neck of the camel or she-camel, and the plural is 'jadl', as it is also said for the male of livestock 'jadīl'. 2 - 'al-jadwal': which is the small river, and it is extended and its water is stronger in the cohesion of its parts than the spreading flood, its plural is 'jadāwil'. 3 - 'al-majdūl': which is the man if he is of slender build without emaciation, and in other words: the slender limb, tightly twisted, and the original meaning in this root is: twisting and joining one thing to another and strengthening it. 4 - 'al-jādil': which is the boy when he becomes strong, and 'al-jādil' from the young of the she-camel: it is the one that has grown strong and walks with its mother. 5 - 'al-judūl': which are the limbs, their singular is 'jadl', and 'judūl' of a human: the bones of the arms and legs, it is said: a leg 'majdūla' and 'jadlā''; beautiful in twisting, and a forearm 'ajdal' likewise. The poet said: 'Then he brought them out, with strong forearms, reddish-brown like the overwhelming lion.' 6 - 'al-mujaddala': which is the armor of firm workmanship, of strong manufacture. Al-Azharī said: 'Armors are called 'jadlā'' and 'mujaddala' due to the firmness of their rings, just as it is said: a twisted rope, and indeed I twisted it firmly.' 7 - 'al-ajdal': the falcon, and it was named so because it is the strongest of birds. The poet said: 'As if the young sandgrouse met a falcon, a hawk.' 8 - 'al-jadāla': which is the firm, even ground, with a hard surface, and therefore it is said: he threw him down 'jadlan' and 'jadalah' so he fell down and was thrown; he threw him on the ground, and he is 'majdūl', and I threw him down 'jadlan', and most often it is said: I threw him down 'tajdīlan', and the one thrown down is called 'majdūl' because he is thrown on the ground. The poet said: ...
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed lexical analysis of the root (ج د ل) by enumerating eight distinct derived words and their meanings. The author begins by stating that the core significance of the root is twisting firmly, then applies this to different objects: a camel's nose-ring (جديل), a small river (جدول) for its cohesive flow, a slender but firm-built man (مجدول), a strong boy or camel calf (جادل), and the limbs of the body (جدول) for their twisted sinews. The same notion of firm construction appears in armor (مجدولة), while the falcon (أجدل) is named for its strength. Finally, the solid ground (جدالة) onto which a person is thrown gives the verb 'to wrestle' (جدل). Through these examples, the author shows that the concrete meanings all revolve around the concept of twisting and strength, which later informs the abstract logical term 'jadal' (dialectic) as a form of rigorous argument.

Key Takeaway
The Arabic root ج د ل signifies twisting and strength, and its concrete derivatives—from rope to river to armor—converge on the idea of firm construction, which later underpins the logical term jadal.

Linguistic Definition of Jadal
Author defines jadal linguistically via Ibn Manzur and Ibn Ashur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the linguistic meaning of jadal from its technical meaning in Islamic logic and identify the etymological emphasis on strength and firmness.

Core Idea
Jadal linguistically means forceful argumentation, not mere discussion.

This lesson examines the linguistic roots of the term 'jadal' (dialectic) as defined by classical lexicographers Ibn Manzur and Ibn Ashur. Understanding this foundation is crucial before exploring its technical usage in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh, where jadal becomes a method of debate based on accepted premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root meaning
Notice that all words derived from the root j-d-l share a core sense of strength, severity, and firmness. This common semantic core underlies the specific definition of jadal.
The root meaning is essential for understanding why jadal is not just any argument.
2
Extract Ibn Manzur's definition
Ibn Manzur defines jadal as 'vehement persistence in quarrel and ability for it.' Look at the Arabic phrase 'al-ladad fi al-khusuma wa al-qudra 'alayha' and note the terms 'ladad' (vehemence) and 'qudra' (ability).
He also lists derived forms: jadal (noun), jadal (active participle), mijdal and mijdal (intensive).
3
Analyze Ibn Ashur's contribution
Ibn Ashur explains that 'mujadala' is a reciprocal verbal noun (muṭālaʿa) requiring two opponents. This is why no bare triliteral verb 'jadala' is used; disputation inherently involves two parties.
He clarifies that 'jadalahu' (he defeated him in debate) is a secondary meaning, not the base form.
4
Distinguish key terms
The passage uses three related terms: al-jadal (the noun for the skill/ability), al-mujadala (the reciprocal debate), and al-jidal (the source noun for disputation). Note their distinct shades of meaning.
In later technical usage, these terms may overlap, but the linguistic distinctions remain important.
5
Prepare for the technical shift
Recognize that the linguistic meaning of forceful argumentation will later be refined in Islamic logic to refer to a specific method using accepted premises (musallamat). This shift does not discard the root meaning but specifies it.
The 'shift_alert' in the key terms highlights this transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : المعنى اللغوي لكلمة الجدل خاصة

إضافة إلى الألفاظ السابقة التي استعملت فيها مادة (ج . د . ل)، والتي رأينا اتفاقها في إفادة معنى جامع هو القوة والشدة والإحكام في الأمر، يأتي اللفظ الذي يهمنا في سياق البحث، وهو كلمة «الجدل» بفتح الجيم والدال، وفي تعريفه لغة، قال ابن منظور : الجدل: اللدد في الخصومة والقدرة عليها، وقد جادله مجادلة وجدالا، ورجل جدل ومجدل ومجدال ؛ شديد الجدل، ويقال : جادلت الرجل فجدلته جدلا، أي غلبته، ورجل جدل إذا كان أقوى في الخصام، وجادله، أي خاصمه، مجادلة وجدالا ، والاسم الجدل وهو شدة الخصومة.

وقال ابن عاشور : المجادلة، مفاعلة من الجدل، وهو القدرة على الخصام والحجة فيه، وهي منازعة بالقول لإقناع الغير برأيك ... ولم يسمع للجدل فرع مجرد أصلي، والمسموع جادل، لأن الخصام يستدعي خصمين، وأما قولهم: جدله، فهو بمعنى غلبه في المجادلة، فليس فعلا أصليا في الاشتقاق. ومصدر المجادلة الجدال، قال تعالى: و... ولا جدال في ...
The second section: The linguistic meaning of the word 'jadal' specifically. In addition to the previous terms in which the root (j-d-l) was used, and which we saw agree in conveying a comprehensive meaning of strength, severity, and firmness in the matter, comes the word that concerns us in the context of the research, which is the word 'al-jadal' (with fath of the jim and dal). And in its linguistic definition, Ibn Manzur said: 'Al-jadal: vehement persistence in quarrel and ability for it. He debated him with debate and disputation. A man is jadal, mijdal, and mijdal: intense in debate. It is said: I debated the man and jadaltuhu (I defeated him) in debate, i.e., I overcame him. A man is jadal if he is stronger in contention. He debated him, i.e., argued with him, with debate and disputation. And the noun is al-jadal, which is intense argumentation.' And Ibn Ashur said: 'Al-mujadala is a verbal noun (muṭālaʿa) from al-jadal, which is the ability for quarrel and argument in it. It is a verbal contest to convince the other of your opinion... No bare original derived form of jadal has been heard; what is heard is jādala, because dispute requires two opponents. As for their saying: jadalahu, it means he overcame him in debate, so it is not an original verbal form in derivation. The source of al-mujadala is al-jidal. Allah said: ...and no jidal in ...'
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the linguistic meaning of 'jadal' (dialectic) by first noting that all words derived from the root j-d-l share a core sense of strength, severity, and firmness. It then focuses on 'al-jadal' specifically, quoting two classical lexicographers. Ibn Manzur defines jadal as 'vehement persistence in quarrel and ability for it,' listing derived forms: the verbal noun jadal, the active participle jadal (a person skilled in debate), and the nouns of intensity mijdal and mijdal. He also notes that 'jadaltuhu' means 'I defeated him in debate,' connecting the root to victory in argument. Ibn Ashur expands by explaining that 'mujadala' is a reciprocal verbal noun (muṭālaʿa) implying two parties, which is why no bare triliteral verb 'jadala' is used—disputation inherently requires two opponents. He clarifies that 'jadalahu' (he overcame him) is secondary and not the base form. The source of 'mujadala' is 'jidal' (disputation), and he cites the Qur'anic verse 'wa lā jidāla fī...' (and no disputation in...), though the quotation is cut off. Overall, the passage establishes that jadal in language means strong, skillful argumentation aimed at victory or persuasion.

Key Terms
الجدل (al-jadal): Vehement persistence in quarrel and ability for it; intense argumentation. [Note: In classical Arabic, jadal primarily denoted strong argumentation; in Islamic logic (mantiq), it becomes a technical term for dialectic as one of the five arts, focusing on premises that are accepted (musallamat) rather than certain.]
المجادلة (al-mujādala): Reciprocal debate from al-jadal; a verbal contest to convince the other of one's opinion. [Note: In Qur'anic usage (e.g., Sūrat al-Mujādala), it refers to pleading or arguing; in later usul al-fiqh, it becomes a method of legal debate.]
الجدال (al-jidāl): Source noun of mujādala; disputation. [Note: Often used interchangeably with jadal in classical texts, but jidāl is more specifically the noun form for the act of disputing.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Manzur, what does 'al-jadal' linguistically mean?
A) Polite discussion
B) Weakness in speech
C) Vehement persistence in quarrel and ability for it
D) A type of poetry

Key Takeaway
The linguistic meaning of jadal emphasizes strength and ability in argument, which forms the foundation for its technical use in Islamic dialectical sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Linguistic Roots of Jadal: Strength and Contention
Author presents three linguistic derivations of jadal and their shared meaning of strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main linguistic derivations of 'jadal' and explain how they converge on the meaning of strong argumentation.

Core Idea
Jadal linguistically means strength and overcoming, like a palace, a stream, or a falcon.

The term 'jadal' is central to Islamic dialectic. Before exploring its technical use in Usul al-Fiqh, we must understand its linguistic roots. The author presents three possible derivations—from 'majdal' (palace), 'jadwal' (stream), and 'ajdal' (falcon)—each emphasizing strength and contention. These derivations converge on a core meaning that shapes its later terminological definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roots
Look for the three etymological suggestions: from 'majdal' (palace), 'jadwal' (stream), and 'ajdal' (falcon). Note the common thread of strength and overcoming.
2
Analyze each metaphor
For each derivation, explain how the metaphor relates to debate: fortification (palace), twisting/overwhelming (stream), pouncing (falcon).
3
Synthesize the common meaning
Recognize that all point to intense, forceful exchange of arguments. Al-Tufi explicitly states that the root j-d-l in all forms returns to strength and resistance.
4
Connect to later definition
See how this linguistic sense prepares for the terminological definition where jadal is about presenting proofs in a structured contest.

الاستعلاء والارتفاع على صاحبه في الحجة حتى يكون منه كموضع الجدال وهو البلح من النخلة . ويمكن اشتقاقه من المجدل، وهو القصر، وجمعه مجادل، لأن كل واحد من المتجادلين يتحصن من صاحبه بالحجة تحصن صاحب القصر به . ويمكن اشتقاقه من الجدول، وهو النهر الصغير لتفتل الماء فيه، فكان كل واحد منهما يقصد قتل صاحبه عن رأيه فتل الماء في النهر. ويمكن اشتقاقه الأجدل من وهو الصقر، كأن كل واحد منهما يسطو بالحجة على صاحبه سطوة الأجدل على الطير ويشتد عليه اشتداده عليها (1). والمهم أن الحالات ينطوي على معنى القوة والمغالبة وشدة المخاصمة وتبادل الحجج والبينات بين طرفين قصد كل واحد منهما الدفاع عن رأيه ومحاولة إقناع خصمه به، أو على الأقل إلحاق الهزيمة به، فهو كما قال الراغب الأصفهاني : «المفاوضة على سبيل المنازعة والمغالبة» (2) ، أو كما قال ابن عاشور: «القدرة على الخصام والحجة فيه»(3) قال الطوفي : وكأن مادة "ج، د، ل" ترجع في جميع تصاريفها إلى معنى القوة والامتناع والشد والإحكام، فيكون الجدل مشتقا من هذا المعنى الجامع الكلي ومن كل واحد من جزئياته باعتبار ما يشتركان فيه من ذلك المعنى (4).
المبحث الثاني التعريف الاصطلاحي للجدل عند الأصوليين المعنى الاصطلاحي لأي لفظ غالبًا ما يقترب من المعنى اللغوي، ولكن - طبعا - مع اكتسابه لعناصر جديدة تتوافق مع طبيعة المجال العلمي الذي يستعمل فيه، وهذه العناصر تتباين بحسب تباين فنون العلم وتنوع موضوعاتها واختلاف مناهجها . وفيما يتعلق بمصطلح الجدل، فإن علماء أصول الفقه حين تناولوه بالتعريف، لم يبتعدوا به عن معناه اللغوي، بل إننا نجد أن كل تعريفاتهم له تدور في دائرة ذلك المعنى اللغوي، وإن كان هنا به بينها نوع من التباين في العناصر التي تضمنتها إلى جانب هذا المعنى اللغوي الأصلي. وقد أوصلنا تتبع مؤلفات الأصوليين إلى الوقوف على جملة من التعريفات الاصطلاحية لكلمة الجدل نسوقها ونعلق عليها فيما يلي :
1 - تعريف ابن حزم الأندلسي (ت: 456 هـ) : عرف الإمام ابن حزم الأندلسي (1) الجدل بقوله : «الجدل والجدال : إخبار كل واحد من المختلفين بحجته أو بما يقدر أنه حجته، وقد يكون أحدهما محقا والآخر مبطلا إما في لفظه، وإما في مراده، أو في كليهما، ولا سبيل أن يكونا معا محقين في ألفاظهما ومعانيهما » (2) .
وتعريف ابن حزم هذا يمكن أن نلاحظ عليه ما يلي.
- أنه يتعلق أساسا بموضوع الجدل، وهو ما يجري بين الطرفين من إظهار الأدلة والحجج .
- أنه يؤكد على أنه لا سبيل إلى أن يكون كلا المتجادلين محقا، فلا بد أن يكون أحدهما محقا والآخر مبطلا ، أو يكونا معا مبطلين، وهذا مهم جدا .
Superiority and elevation over one's opponent in argument until he becomes like a place of contention, and it is the unripe date from the palm tree. And it can be derived from 'majdal', which is a palace, and its plural is 'majadil', because each of the two disputants fortifies himself against his companion with an argument like the owner of the palace fortifies himself. And it can be derived from 'jadwal', which is a small river, for the twisting of water in it; so each of them aims to overwhelm his companion's opinion like the twisting of water in the river. And it can be derived from 'ajdal', which is the falcon, as if each of them pounces on his companion with an argument like the pouncing of the falcon on birds and intensifies against him like its intensification upon them (1). And the important thing is that the states involve the meaning of strength and overcoming, intensity of disputation, and exchange of arguments and evidences between two parties, each aiming to defend his opinion and try to convince his opponent with it, or at least to defeat him. So it is as al-Raghib al-Isfahani said: 'Negotiation by way of contest and overcoming' (2), or as Ibn Ashur said: 'Ability in disputation and argument' (3). Al-Tufi said: 'The root j-d-l in all its derivatives returns to the meaning of strength, resistance, intensity, and firmness; thus jadal is derived from this comprehensive universal meaning and from each of its particulars by virtue of what they share of that meaning' (4).
The second section: The terminological definition of jadal among the Usuliyyun. The terminological meaning of any word often approaches its linguistic meaning, but – of course – with the acquisition of new elements that correspond to the nature of the scientific field in which it is used. And these elements differ according to the difference of the disciplines of knowledge, the diversity of their subjects, and the difference of their methodologies. Regarding the term jadal, the scholars of Usul al-Fiqh, when they dealt with its definition, did not depart from its linguistic meaning; rather we find that all their definitions revolve within the circle of that linguistic meaning, although there is among them a kind of variation in the elements included alongside this original linguistic meaning. And our examination of the works of the Usuliyyun has led us to find a group of terminological definitions of the word jadal, which we present and comment on as follows:
1 - The definition of Ibn Hazm al-Andalusi (d. 456 AH): Imam Ibn Hazm al-Andalusi (1) defined jadal by saying: 'Jadal and jidal: the informing by each of the two disputants of his proof or what he supposes to be his proof. One of them may be truthful and the other false, either in his wording, or in his intended meaning, or in both; and it is impossible for them both to be truthful in their words and meanings' (2).
And upon this definition of Ibn Hazm, we can note the following:
- That it relates primarily to the subject of jadal, which is the presentation of evidences and arguments between the two parties.
- That it emphasizes that it is impossible for both disputants to be truthful; rather one must be truthful and the other false, or both be false. And this is very important.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage provides both a linguistic exploration of the word 'jadal' and then introduces the terminological definition as understood by scholars of Usul al-Fiqh, beginning with Ibn Hazm. The author first presents several possible linguistic derivations: from the sense of superiority and elevation, from 'majdal' (palace) indicating mutual fortification, from 'jadwal' (small stream) suggesting twisting and overwhelming, and from 'ajdal' (falcon) symbolizing forceful pouncing. These converge on a core meaning of strength, resistance, and intense argumentation. The author then notes that the terminological definitions by Usul scholars remain close to this linguistic root. The first such definition is from Ibn Hazm, who defines jadal as each disputant presenting his proof (or what he takes to be proof). Ibn Hazm stresses that the two parties cannot both be correct — at most one is correct, or both are wrong. Two observations are made: first, the definition centers on the exchange of evidences; second, it asserts the impossibility of both being truthful, which is crucial for understanding the nature of debate in Islamic legal theory.

جدال
مجادلة

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a linguistic derivation of 'jadal' mentioned in the passage?
A) From 'majdal' (palace)
B) From 'jadwal' (stream)
C) From 'ajdal' (falcon)
D) From 'jabal' (mountain)

Key Takeaway
The linguistic origins of jadal consistently evoke strength and overcoming, preparing us for its technical use as a structured debate where each side presents proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another Islamic term whose linguistic root informs its technical meaning (e.g., 'ijtihad' from j-h-d meaning effort). How does the root meaning shape the term in Usul al-Fiqh?

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
7 min
Ibn Hazm's Terminological Definition of Jadal
Ibn Hazm's terminological definition of jadal with emphasis on the impossibility of both parties being correct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Ibn Hazm's definition of jadal and explain why he asserts that both disputants cannot be correct.

Core Idea
In Ibn Hazm's view, jadal is the exchange of proofs, but at most one party can be right.

After the linguistic exploration, the author turns to the terminological definition of jadal given by Ibn Hazm. Ibn Hazm defines jadal as each disputant informing the other of his proof (or supposed proof). Critically, he adds that it is impossible for both to be correct—one may be right, the other wrong, or both wrong. This epistemological constraint is central to the Islamic understanding of debate: jadal is not about relativism but about seeking truth.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Hazm's definition
Locate the sentence: 'al-jadal wa al-jidal: ikhbār kulli wāḥid min al-mukhtalifayn bi-ḥujjatihi...' Understand that jadal is defined as an act of informing (ikhbār) about a proof (ḥujja).
2
Identify the key components
Note that the definition includes two elements: the presentation of proof by each disputant, and the possibility that it may be a genuine proof or merely supposed proof.
3
Examine the truth condition
Ibn Hazm explicitly states that it is impossible for both disputants to be truthful. Understand the logical implication: either one is correct and the other false, or both are false, but never both correct.
4
Reflect on the significance
This condition establishes jadal as a truth-seeking activity, not a relativistic exchange. The goal is to identify the correct position through rigorous argument.

What Is Mantiq?
