المبحث الأول
النظر العقلي
حديثنا عن النظر العقلي، سينصب حول تعريفه ثم بيان أهميته في الجدل الأصولي، وكذا تحديد شروط سلامته التي تتيح تحقيق نتائج سليمة من خلاله، ثم إبراز نتيجته التي ينتهي إليها والمتمثلة في القطع أو الظن .
المطلب الأول : مفهوم النظر العقلي
النظر العقلي ؛ مصطلح مركب من لفظتين اثنتين النظر» و«العقل»، ويقتضينا تحديد مفهوم هذا المركب أن نحدد مفهومي اللفظتين اللتين يتركب منهما، ولذلك فنحن مطالبون بتعريف مصطلح «النظر ، ثم مصطلح «العقل»، كلا منهما في اللغة والاصطلاح، ثم بعد ذلك تحديد مفهوم النظر العقلي الذي نريده في بحثنا هذا .
أ - النظر في اللغة والاصطلاح
يفيد «النظر في اللغة العربية معنى أصليا واحدا، هو «تأمل الشيء ومعاينته»(1)، وهذا المعنى هو الذي يُستعار في الاستعمال الواقعي، حيث يطلق النظر على أحد معنيين؛ حسي، أو معنوي .
فيراد بالنظر ؛ تقليب البصر لإدراك الشيء ورؤيته ؛ فهو حس العين، ويقع على الأجسام، فإذا قلت : نظرت إليه، لم يكن إلا بالعين)؛ ومنه قوله تعالى : {وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ} كما يراد به كذلك التأمل والفحص والتدبر، فهو الفكر في الشيء تقدره وتقيسه منك (1) أو هو طلب المعنى بالقلب من جهة الفكر (2)، فهو هنا لا يقع بالبصر وإنما بالبصيرة، وفي هذا المعنى قوله تعالى : {أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ}، وقوله : {أَوَلَمْ يَنظُرُوا فِي مَلَكُوتِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ} (4) ، أي ألم يتأملوا، ففي الآيتين حث على التأمل والنظر والتدبر في حكمة الله خلقه واستعمال النظر في البصر أكثر عند العامة، وفي البصيرة أكثر عند الخاصة كما يستعمل النظر إلى جانب هذين المعنيين لإفادة معان أخرى مجازية، فيقال للمعرفة الحاصلة بعد التأمل ؛ نظر (6) ، وذلك من باب إطلاق السبب وإرادة المسبب، كما يقال للانتظار نظر، وللرحمة والتعطف نظر، وللعناية بالغير فيما يحتاج إليه نظر وللمقابلة ،نظر، كما يقال : باب فلان ينظر إلى ذلك الباب، وهذا الجبل ينظر إلى ذلك الجبل، إذا تقابلا . . . والمعنى المقصود في بحثنا هنا هو النظر بمعنى التأمل والتدبر والتفكر، لا غيره من المعاني الأخرى.
هذا في اللغة . . وأما في الاصطلاح، فالبحث والاستقراء يوقفنا على تعاريف كثيرة للنظر عند علماء أصول الفقه، وهذه التعاريف قد تتفق وقد تختلف في بعض العناصر .
من هذه التعاريف قول إمام الحرمين الجويني :
وحقيقة النظر هاهنا ؛ فكر القلب وتأمله في حال المنظور، ليعرف حكمه جميعا أو فرقا أو تقسيما. وحقيقة هذا النظر هو : التأمل، أو التفكر، أو التدبر، أو الاعتبار، أو
First Section: Rational Inquiry. Our discussion of rational inquiry will revolve around its definition, then explaining its importance in usul dialectic, as well as determining the conditions for its soundness that allow achieving sound results through it, then highlighting its result which it reaches, represented by certainty or assumption. First Demand: The Concept of Rational Inquiry. Rational inquiry: a compound term composed of two words: 'inquiry' and 'intellect'. Defining this compound requires us to define the concepts of the two words that compose it. Therefore we are required to define the term 'inquiry', then the term 'intellect', each in language and terminology, then after that defining the concept of rational inquiry that we intend in this research. A - Inquiry in Language and Terminology. 'Inquiry' in the Arabic language indicates one primary meaning, which is 'contemplation and inspection of a thing'. This meaning is what is borrowed in actual usage, where inquiry is used for one of two meanings: sensory or intellectual. By inquiry is meant: turning the eye to perceive and see a thing; it is the sense of sight, and it applies to bodies. If you say: 'I looked at him', it is only with the eye. From this is His statement: 'Faces that Day will be radiant, looking at their Lord'. Likewise it is also meant as contemplation, examination, and reflection. It is thought about a thing, estimating and gauging it from yourself, or it is seeking meaning with the heart through thought. Here it does not occur with sight but with insight. In this sense is His statement: 'Do they not look at the camels, how they are created?', and His statement: 'Do they not look at the dominion of the heavens and the earth?', i.e., did they not contemplate. In both verses there is encouragement to contemplate, look, and reflect on the wisdom of Allah in His creation. The use of inquiry for sight is more common among the general public, and for insight among the elite. Besides these two meanings, inquiry is also used to convey other metaphorical meanings. Thus the knowledge obtained after contemplation is called 'inquiry', as a case of naming the cause and intending the effect. Likewise, waiting is called inquiry, mercy and affection are called inquiry, care for others in what they need is called inquiry, and facing is called inquiry. It is said: 'So-and-so's door looks at that door' and 'this mountain looks at that mountain' when they face each other. The intended meaning in our research here is inquiry in the sense of contemplation, reflection, and thinking, not any other meanings. This in language. As for terminology, research and induction bring us to many definitions of inquiry among scholars of usul al-fiqh. These definitions may agree or differ in some elements. Among these definitions is the saying of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni: 'The reality of inquiry here is: the heart's thought and contemplation regarding the state of the object of inquiry, to know its ruling collectively, differentially, or divisively. The reality of this inquiry is: contemplation, or reflection, or pondering, or taking heed, or...'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage opens the discussion on rational inquiry (al-nazar al-'aqli) in the context of usul dialectic. The author first states the plan: defining it, showing its importance, conditions for soundness, and its result (certainty or assumption). Then he begins the first demand: the concept of rational inquiry. Since the term is compound ('inquiry' and 'intellect'), he first defines each component. Here he defines 'inquiry' (al-nazar) in language and terminology. Linguistically, nazar has one primary meaning: 'contemplation and inspection' (ta'ammul al-shay' wa mu'ayanatuh). From this, it is used in two main senses: sensory (turning the eye to see) and intellectual (contemplation with the mind). He cites Quranic verses: 'Faces looking at their Lord' (75:22-23) for the sensory, and 'Do they not look at the camels...' (88:17) and 'Do they not look at the dominion...' (7:185) for the intellectual, showing that the latter means 'did they not reflect'. He notes that sensory usage is more common among the general public, while intellectual usage is among the elite. Additionally, nazar has metaphorical meanings: knowledge after contemplation, waiting, mercy, care, and facing. The author clarifies that in this research, the intended meaning is intellectual contemplation, not the others. Then he moves to terminology: scholars of usul al-fiqh have many definitions, which may agree or differ. He gives an example from al-Juwayni, who defines nazar as the heart's thought and contemplation about an object to know its ruling (collectively, differentially, or divisively). Al-Juwayni equates it with contemplation, reflection, pondering, taking heed, etc., but the passage cuts off. This definition emphasizes that nazar is an intellectual act aiming at deriving a ruling (hukm) in usul al-fiqh. The example of al-Juwayni is classic: nazar is not mere sight but mental examination to infer legal rulings.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar): In this passage, nazar is defined linguistically as ‘contemplation and inspection’ (تأمل الشيء ومعاينته), used both for sensory sight (حسي) and intellectual insight (معنوي). Terminologically, according to al-Juwayni, it is ‘the heart’s thought and contemplation regarding the state of the object of inquiry to know its ruling’ (فكر القلب وتأمله في حال المنظور، ليعرف حكمه). [Note: No significant shift — nazar is stable across usul al-fiqh as rational inquiry to derive rulings.]
العقل (al-'aql): Only mentioned as part of the compound ‘al-nazar al-‘aqli’, but not defined yet in this passage. It will be defined later. [Note: Definition deferred in this passage.]
الجدل الأصولي (al-jadal al-usuli): The dialectic method used in usul al-fiqh; the context in which rational inquiry is applied. [Note: No significant shift — jadal al-usuli is the standard term for dialectical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Juwayni as cited in the passage, what is the reality of nazar (inquiry) in usul al-fiqh?
A) Turning the eye to perceive physical objects
B) The heart's thought and contemplation regarding the object of inquiry to know its ruling
C) Waiting for something to happen
D) Showing mercy to someone

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni defines nazar as the heart's thought and contemplation on the object of inquiry to know its ruling, establishing it as an intellectual act of inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using al-Juwayni's structure, write a definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) in one sentence: 'It is the heart's thought and contemplation regarding the original case (asl) and the new case (far') to extend the ruling.'

Linguistic and Technical Meanings of Nazar
Author defines 'nazar' in language and terminology, citing multiple usuli scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic (lughawi) and technical (istilahi) meanings of nazar and identify which is used in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Nazar in usul is exclusively mental reflection, not sensory sight.

You may have encountered the word 'nazar' in everyday Arabic meaning 'sight' or 'looking'. But when usuli scholars speak of 'nazar', they mean something more precise: the mental act of reasoning from evidence to a conclusion. In this lesson, you'll learn how the technical definition narrows the linguistic one and why that matters for legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings mentioned
The passage says nazar in language includes both sensory (حسي) and mental (معنوي) meanings. Write down what each could be.
Sensory meaning is seeing with eyes; mental meaning is thinking/reflecting.
2
Find the restriction in technical usage
The author says in terminology it is used only for the mental meaning. Compare it to the linguistic scope. How is it more specific?
A specialty (ikhtisas) restricts the general.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
If someone says 'nazartu fi al-dalil', do they mean they looked at the text with their eyes or they reflected on it? Which meaning is operative in usul?
Usuli usage is always mental.
4
Explain why the restriction matters
Why would usul al-fiqh need a precise term for mental reasoning?
To avoid confusion with physical sight when discussing evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل واحد من هذا يصلح أن يكون حدًا لما نعنيه بالنظر هاهنا (1)
وقال أبو مظفر السمعاني : النظر: هو الفكر في حال المنظور إليه، والتوصل بأدلته إلى المطلوب (2) . وعرف ابن عقيل الحنبلي النظر بأنه : التأمل والتفكر والاعتبار لمعرفة الحق من الباطل، والفصل بين الحجة والشبهة (3) .
فالنظر - إذا - في الاصطلاح هو أخص من النظر في اللغة، فإذا كان النظر في اللغة يطلق على المعنى الحسي والمعنوي ،معا فإنه في الاصطلاح يطلق على المعنوي فقط، والمراد بالنظر الوصول عن طريق التأمل في الأدلة إلى معرفة حكم مسألة ما أو أقسامها وكل ما يتعلق بها مما يهم الناظر معرفته معرفة تفصل بين الحق والباطل فيه .
And each one of these can be a definition of what we mean by 'nazar' here (1). Abu Muzaffar al-Sam'ani said: 'Nazar: it is reflection (fikr) on the state of the object of examination, and arriving through its evidence to the desired conclusion' (2). And Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali defined nazar as: 'contemplation, reflection, and consideration to know truth from falsehood, and to distinguish between proof and doubt' (3). So nazar – therefore – in terminology is more specific than nazar in language, for whereas nazar in language is used for both sensory and mental meaning collectively, in terminology it is used only for the mental meaning. And what is meant by nazar is to reach, by way of reflection on evidence, knowledge of the ruling of a certain issue or its divisions and everything pertaining to it that the examiner cares to know, with a knowledge that distinguishes between truth and falsehood therein.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'nazar' (speculation/reasoning) as used in the science of usul al-fiqh and jadal. It opens by referring to previous definitions and then provides three distinct yet complementary definitions from major usuli scholars. The first definition by al-Sam'ani emphasizes nazar as a mental act of reflecting on the object of inquiry and using its proofs to reach the sought conclusion. The second by Ibn 'Aqil adds the elements of distinguishing truth from falsehood and proof from doubt. The passage then clarifies the relationship between the linguistic and technical meanings: in language, nazar can refer to both physical sight and mental reflection, but in technical usage it is restricted to the mental act of reasoning through evidence. The goal of nazar is to obtain certain knowledge of a ruling or classification, separating truth from falsehood. All examples used are from Islamic legal theory, not secular reasoning.

Key Terms
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the linguistic meaning of nazar and its technical meaning in usul al-fiqh?
A) They are identical
B) The technical is broader
C) The technical is more specific
D) They have no relation

Key Takeaway
Nazar in usul al-fiqh is restricted to mental reflection, excluding physical sight, to precisely denote reasoning from evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Usuli Definition of Nazar: Reflection on Evidence to Discern Truth
Author defines 'nazar' in language and terminology, citing multiple usuli scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core components of the usuli definition of nazar and explain its purpose in distinguishing truth from falsehood.

Core Idea
Nazar is the mental act that uses evidence to separate truth from falsehood.

The usuli scholars didn't just define nazar as any thinking – they gave it a specific function: to arrive at knowledge of rulings by reflecting on evidence, and to distinguish between proof and doubt. In this lesson, we'll break down the definitions from al-Sam'ani and Ibn 'Aqil to understand what nazar means in practice.

Guided Path
1
Extract the key elements from al-Sam'ani's definition
He says nazar is: (a) reflection (fikr), (b) on the state of the object (hal al-manzur ilayh), (c) arriving through its evidence (bi-adillatihi) to the desired conclusion (al-matlub). List these components.
2
Extract the key elements from Ibn 'Aqil's definition
He says nazar is: (a) contemplation, reflection, consideration (ta'mmul, tafakkur, i'tibar), (b) to know truth from falsehood (li-ma'rifat al-haqq min al-batil), (c) to distinguish between proof and doubt (al-fasl bayn al-hujja wa al-shubha). Compare with al-Sam'ani's.
3
Identify the common purpose
Both definitions aim at reaching knowledge (ma'rifa) and distinguishing truth/falsehood. What is the outcome of nazar according to the passage?
It is to reach knowledge of a ruling or its divisions, with a distinction between truth and falsehood.
4
Synthesize a single definition
Combine the elements: Nazar is the mental act of reflecting on evidence to derive knowledge that separates truth from falsehood. This is the working definition for usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Linguistic Roots of 'Aql
Author explores linguistic definitions of 'aql

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the etymological derivation of 'aql and relate it to its lexical meanings.

Core Idea
The word 'aql literally means 'restraint'—the intellect restrains you from ruin.

The author begins by grounding 'aql in its Arabic root. Understanding this linguistic foundation is essential because the technical term builds on it.

Guided Path
1
Trace the root
Identify that 'aql is derived from 'aqala meaning 'to restrain' or 'to hold back'. This is shown by the phrase 'because it restrains its possessor from falling into perils'.
2
Identify the lexical meaning
Note the second lexical meaning given: 'aql is the discrimination (tamyiz) by which man is distinguished from animals. This is a cognitive sense.
3
Recognize related phrases
Examine the phrase 'qalb 'aqul' (discerning heart) and 'aqala al-shay'a' (he understood it). See how the same root carries the sense of understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل عقلا، لأنه يعقل صاحبه عن التورط في المهالك، أي يحبسه وقيل : العقل هو التمييز الذي يتميز به الإنسان من سائر الحيوان ويقال : لفلان قلب عقول ولسان سؤول، وقلب عقول : فهم، وعقل الشيء يعقله عقلا؛ فهمه. أما في الاصطلاح، فقد اختلف العلماء في تعريف العقل وحقيقته اختلافا كثيرا ; ولعل أجمع وأدق ما يقال فيه : قول الغزالي ومن وافقه بعدم إمكان حده بحد واحد يحيط به، لأنه يطلق بالاشتراك على خمسة معان: أحدها: إطلاقه على الغريزة التي يتهيأ بها الإنسان درك العلوم النظرية وتدبير الأمور الخفية . والثاني : إطلاقه على بعض الأمور الضرورية وهي التي تخرج إلى الوجود في ذات الطفل المميز بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات . والثالث : إطلاقه على العلوم المستفادة من التجربة فإن من حنكته التجارب يقال عنه إنه عاقل، ومن لا يتصف بذلك يقال عنه غبي جاهل. والرابع : إطلاقه على ما يوصل إلى ثمرة معرفة عواقب الأمور، بقمع الشهوات الداعية إلى اللذات العاجلة التي تعقبها الندامة ; فإذا حصلت هذه القوة سمي صاحبها عاقلا. والخامس : إطلاقه على الهدوء والوقار، وهي هيئة محمودة للإنسان في حركاته وكلامه، فيقال : فلان عاقل أي عنده هدوء ورزانة . وقد ذكر الراغب الأصفهاني وغيره أن العقل يطلق على القوة المتهيئة لقبول العلم، كما يقال للعلم الذي يستفيده الإنسان بتلك القوة، فكل موضع ذم الله الكفار بعدم العقل، فأشار إلى الثاني، وكل موضع رفع التكليف عن العبد لعدم العقل، فأشار إلى الأول . والذي نعنيه بالعقل في هذا المقام : أداة التمييز والإدراك التي يتمكن بها الإنسان من تمييز المعلومات بعضها عن بعض وتوليد بعضها من بعض، وهنا يمكن أن نورد تعريف السمعاني للعقل، حيث يعرفه بأنه: «قوة ضرورية بوجودها درك الأشياء، ويتوجه تكليف الشرع، وهو ما يعرفه كل إنسان من نفسه، ولا يستدل عليه بغيره لأن الاستدلال مفتقر إلى علم ينظر فيه وأصل يعتمد عليه، ولو كان غيره دليلا عليه لكان مكتسبا لا ضروريًا»
Al-'Aql is derived from 'aqala, meaning to restrain, because it restrains its possessor from falling into perils, i.e., it holds him back. It is said: al-'aql is the discrimination by which man is distinguished from other animals. It is said: So-and-so has a discerning heart (qalb 'aqul) and an inquiring tongue. Qalb 'aqul means understanding. 'Aqala al-shay'a ya'qiluhu 'aqlan means he understood it. As for terminological usage, the scholars have differed greatly in defining 'aql and its reality. Perhaps the most comprehensive and precise statement about it is that of al-Ghazali and those who agree with him, that it cannot be encompassed by a single definition because it is used homonymously for five meanings: First: its use for the innate disposition (al-ghariza) by which man becomes prepared to grasp theoretical sciences and manage subtle affairs. Second: its use for some necessary matters that emerge in the discerning child regarding the possibility of possible things and impossibility of impossible things. Third: its use for knowledge acquired from experience, for one who is seasoned by experiences is called 'intelligent', and one who does not possess that is called foolish and ignorant. Fourth: its use for that which leads to the fruit of knowing the outcomes of affairs, by suppressing desires that call to immediate pleasures which are followed by regret; when this power is obtained, its possessor is called 'intelligent'. Fifth: its use for calmness and dignity, which is a praiseworthy comportment in one's movements and speech; so it is said: So-and-so is 'intelligent', meaning he has calmness and solidity. Al-Raghib al-Isfahani and others mentioned that 'aql is applied to the power prepared to receive knowledge, just as it is said for the knowledge that man acquires by that power. So wherever Allah blames the disbelievers for lack of 'aql, it refers to the second; and wherever legal responsibility is lifted from the servant due to lack of 'aql, it refers to the first. What we mean by 'aql in this context is: the instrument of discrimination and perception by which man is able to distinguish bits of information from one another and generate some from others. Here we can quote al-Sam'ani's definition of 'aql, where he defines it as: 'A necessary power by which things are perceived, and the legal obligation (taklif) of the Shari'a is addressed; it is what every person knows of himself and is not inferred from something else, because inference is dependent on knowledge to be considered and a foundation to rely upon, and if something else were a proof for it, it would be acquired, not necessary.'
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive lexical and terminological definition of 'aql (intellect/reason). The author begins by tracing the Arabic root 'aqala, meaning 'to restrain' or 'to hold back', noting that the intellect is so named because it restrains its possessor from falling into perils. He then gives additional linguistic senses: 'aql as discrimination distinguishing humans from animals, and the phrase 'qalb 'aqul' (discerning heart). He then moves to the technical usage, highlighting scholarly disagreement. The author presents al-Ghazali's position that 'aql cannot be captured by a single definition because it is homonymous across five distinct meanings. These five are enumerated: (1) innate disposition for theoretical knowledge and subtle management, (2) necessary knowledge evident in the discerning child regarding possibility and impossibility, (3) experiential knowledge gained from life experience, (4) practical wisdom that controls desires and considers consequences, and (5) dignified calmness in demeanor. The author then notes al-Raghib al-Isfahani's distinction between the power itself and the knowledge it acquires, correlating with Qur'anic blame (second meaning) versus removal of legal responsibility (first meaning). Finally, the author adopts a specific definition for this work: 'aql as 'the instrument of discrimination and perception' enabling distinction and generation of knowledge, reinforcing it with al-Sam'ani's definition of 'aql as a necessary, self-evident power foundational for taklif. The passage thus sets the groundwork for later discussion of rational consideration (nazar) in usul al-fiqh.

Key Takeaway
The Arabic root of 'aql carries a primary sense of restraint, which underlies its later intellectual meanings.

The Five Meanings of 'Aql according to al-Ghazali
Author outlines Ghazali's five homonymous meanings of 'aql

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the five homonymous meanings of 'aql as enumerated by al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali shows that 'aql is not one thing—it is a homonym with five distinct senses.

After the linguistic definition, the author presents al-Ghazali's position that 'aql cannot be captured by a single definition. This explains why scholars differ on its nature and scope.

Guided Path
1
Understand the homonymy claim
Note that al-Ghazali argues 'aql is used homonymously (ishtirak) for five distinct meanings, so no single definition covers all uses.
2
Enumerate the five meanings
List them as given: (1) innate disposition (ghariza), (2) necessary knowledge in the discerning child, (3) experiential knowledge, (4) practical wisdom that controls desires, (5) calmness and dignity.
3
Recognize the textual evidence for each
For each meaning, see the example or description provided by the author. Note that meanings 1 and 2 are linked to taklif and Qur'anic blame.
4
See the Raghib's refinement
Al-Raghib al-Isfahani adds that 'aql can refer to the power or the knowledge acquired; this clarifies the usage in revelation and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل عقلا، لأنه يعقل صاحبه عن التورط في المهالك، أي يحبسه وقيل : العقل هو التمييز الذي يتميز به الإنسان من سائر الحيوان ويقال : لفلان قلب عقول ولسان سؤول، وقلب عقول : فهم، وعقل الشيء يعقله عقلا؛ فهمه. أما في الاصطلاح، فقد اختلف العلماء في تعريف العقل وحقيقته اختلافا كثيرا ; ولعل أجمع وأدق ما يقال فيه : قول الغزالي ومن وافقه بعدم إمكان حده بحد واحد يحيط به، لأنه يطلق بالاشتراك على خمسة معان: أحدها: إطلاقه على الغريزة التي يتهيأ بها الإنسان درك العلوم النظرية وتدبير الأمور الخفية . والثاني : إطلاقه على بعض الأمور الضرورية وهي التي تخرج إلى الوجود في ذات الطفل المميز بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات . والثالث : إطلاقه على العلوم المستفادة من التجربة فإن من حنكته التجارب يقال عنه إنه عاقل، ومن لا يتصف بذلك يقال عنه غبي جاهل. والرابع : إطلاقه على ما يوصل إلى ثمرة معرفة عواقب الأمور، بقمع الشهوات الداعية إلى اللذات العاجلة التي تعقبها الندامة ; فإذا حصلت هذه القوة سمي صاحبها عاقلا. والخامس : إطلاقه على الهدوء والوقار، وهي هيئة محمودة للإنسان في حركاته وكلامه، فيقال : فلان عاقل أي عنده هدوء ورزانة . وقد ذكر الراغب الأصفهاني وغيره أن العقل يطلق على القوة المتهيئة لقبول العلم، كما يقال للعلم الذي يستفيده الإنسان بتلك القوة، فكل موضع ذم الله الكفار بعدم العقل، فأشار إلى الثاني، وكل موضع رفع التكليف عن العبد لعدم العقل، فأشار إلى الأول . والذي نعنيه بالعقل في هذا المقام : أداة التمييز والإدراك التي يتمكن بها الإنسان من تمييز المعلومات بعضها عن بعض وتوليد بعضها من بعض، وهنا يمكن أن نورد تعريف السمعاني للعقل، حيث يعرفه بأنه: «قوة ضرورية بوجودها درك الأشياء، ويتوجه تكليف الشرع، وهو ما يعرفه كل إنسان من نفسه، ولا يستدل عليه بغيره لأن الاستدلال مفتقر إلى علم ينظر فيه وأصل يعتمد عليه، ولو كان غيره دليلا عليه لكان مكتسبا لا ضروريًا»
Al-'Aql is derived from 'aqala, meaning to restrain, because it restrains its possessor from falling into perils, i.e., it holds him back. It is said: al-'aql is the discrimination by which man is distinguished from other animals. It is said: So-and-so has a discerning heart (qalb 'aqul) and an inquiring tongue. Qalb 'aqul means understanding. 'Aqala al-shay'a ya'qiluhu 'aqlan means he understood it. As for terminological usage, the scholars have differed greatly in defining 'aql and its reality. Perhaps the most comprehensive and precise statement about it is that of al-Ghazali and those who agree with him, that it cannot be encompassed by a single definition because it is used homonymously for five meanings: First: its use for the innate disposition (al-ghariza) by which man becomes prepared to grasp theoretical sciences and manage subtle affairs. Second: its use for some necessary matters that emerge in the discerning child regarding the possibility of possible things and impossibility of impossible things. Third: its use for knowledge acquired from experience, for one who is seasoned by experiences is called 'intelligent', and one who does not possess that is called foolish and ignorant. Fourth: its use for that which leads to the fruit of knowing the outcomes of affairs, by suppressing desires that call to immediate pleasures which are followed by regret; when this power is obtained, its possessor is called 'intelligent'. Fifth: its use for calmness and dignity, which is a praiseworthy comportment in one's movements and speech; so it is said: So-and-so is 'intelligent', meaning he has calmness and solidity. Al-Raghib al-Isfahani and others mentioned that 'aql is applied to the power prepared to receive knowledge, just as it is said for the knowledge that man acquires by that power. So wherever Allah blames the disbelievers for lack of 'aql, it refers to the second; and wherever legal responsibility is lifted from the servant due to lack of 'aql, it refers to the first. What we mean by 'aql in this context is: the instrument of discrimination and perception by which man is able to distinguish bits of information from one another and generate some from others. Here we can quote al-Sam'ani's definition of 'aql, where he defines it as: 'A necessary power by which things are perceived, and the legal obligation (taklif) of the Shari'a is addressed; it is what every person knows of himself and is not inferred from something else, because inference is dependent on knowledge to be considered and a foundation to rely upon, and if something else were a proof for it, it would be acquired, not necessary.'
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive lexical and terminological definition of 'aql (intellect/reason). The author begins by tracing the Arabic root 'aqala, meaning 'to restrain' or 'to hold back', noting that the intellect is so named because it restrains its possessor from falling into perils. The Adopted Definition of 'Aql in Usul al-Fiqh
Author adopts a specific definition of 'aql and cites al-Sam'ani

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's adopted definition of 'aql and compare it with al-Sam'ani's definition.

Core Idea
The author defines 'aql as the instrument of discrimination—the tool for generating knowledge from experience.

After presenting the five meanings, the author specifies which meaning he adopts for his work and reinforces it with al-Sam'ani's necessary power definition. This sets the stage for using 'aql as the foundation for nazar (rational consideration) in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's definition
Locate the sentence: 'والذي نعنيه بالعقل في هذا المقام : أداة التمييز والإدراك' (What we mean by 'aql in this context is: the instrument of discrimination and perception).
2
Analyze the components
Note that genus is 'instrument' (tool) and differentia is 'discrimination and perception'. This definition emphasizes function over essence.
3
Examine al-Sam'ani's definition
Al-Sam'ani defines 'aql as a 'necessary power' (quwwa daruriyya) by which things are perceived, and it is self-evident, not inferred.
This highlights that 'aql is foundational and immediate.
4
Compare the two definitions
Both define 'aql as a cognitive tool/faculty, but the author focuses on its operational role (instrument), while al-Sam'ani stresses its necessity and self-evidence.

Defining 'Aql: The Self-Evident Faculty
Author defines 'aql, quoting al-Sam'ani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Sam'ani's definition of 'aql as a necessary, self-evident faculty and justify why it cannot be known through inference.

Core Idea
'Aql is the basis of all knowledge and cannot be proven by inference.

The author begins by defining intellect ('aql) as the tool of distinction and perception. He quotes the Shafi'i usuli al-Sam'ani, who defines 'aql as a necessary faculty that is self-evident and not derived from inference. This definition is crucial because it establishes 'aql as the foundation for legal obligation and the starting point for all rational inquiry in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Find the target definition
Locate the sentence beginning with 'What we mean by intellect in this context'. Identify that this is the author's working definition, followed by al-Sam'ani's more formal definition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia in al-Sam'ani's definition
Al-Sam'ani defines 'aql as a 'necessary faculty' (quwwa daruriyya). The genus is 'faculty'; the differentia is 'necessary' and 'by which things are perceived'.
Note: Here 'necessary' means it is inherent and not acquired through inference.
3
Understand why 'aql is self-evident
Al-Sam'ani argues that inference (istidlal) depends on prior knowledge and a basis. If 'aql were known by inference, it would be acquired, not necessary. Therefore, 'aql must be known directly (badihi).
This is a key argument: the instrument of inference cannot itself be inferred.
4
Apply the definition to jadal usuli
Recognize that this definition of 'aql underlies all subsequent discussion: the intellect is the tool used in rational debate about usul rulings. Without this shared starting point, argument would be impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي نعنيه بالعقل في هذا المقام : أداة التمييز والإدراك التي يتمكن بها الإنسان من تمييز المعلومات بعضها عن بعض وتوليد بعضها من بعض، وهنا يمكن أن نورد تعريف السمعاني للعقل، حيث يعرفه بأنه: «قوة ضرورية بوجودها درك الأشياء، ويتوجه تكليف الشرع، وهو ما يعرفه كل إنسان من نفسه، ولا يستدل عليه بغيره لأن الاستدلال مفتقر إلى علم ينظر فيه وأصل يعتمد عليه، ولو كان غيره دليلا عليه لكان مكتسبا لا ضروريًا» (1) . ج - النظر العقلي : بعد هذا يمكن أن نقول بأن النظر العقلي الذي نريده في هذا البحث هو : تأمل الإنسان وتفكره بعقله في علوم الشرع، من جهة النظر في شروط الاجتهاد وقواعده وسبل الاجتهاد ومسالكه ونتائجه» . أما النظر العقلي في أصول الفقه، فنعني به البحث الفكري والتأمل العقلي الأدلة لاستخلاص القواعد الأصولية، وتحقيقها، ودفع ما قد ينقضها، وبيان حدودها، ومجال عمل كل منها .
What we mean by intellect in this context: the tool of distinction and perception by which a person can distinguish pieces of information from each other and generate some from others. Here we can mention al-Sam'ani's definition of intellect, as he defines it: 'A necessary faculty by which things are perceived, and by which the obligation of the Law is directed, and which every person knows from himself, and is not inferred from something else, because inference depends on knowledge to be examined and a basis to rely on, and if something else were a proof for it, it would be acquired, not necessary.' (1) C. Rational Consideration: After this, we can say that the rational consideration we intend in this research is: a person's reflection and thinking with his intellect in the sciences of the Law, from the perspective of examining the conditions of ijtihad, its rules, means, methods, and results. As for rational consideration in usul al-fiqh, we mean by it intellectual investigation and rational reflection on the evidence to extract usul rules, verify them, repel anything that may refute them, clarify their limits, and the scope of each.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: 'aql (intellect) and nazar 'aqli (rational consideration). The author first presents a working definition of intellect as a tool for distinguishing and generating knowledge, then quotes al-Sam'ani's more precise definition: intellect is a necessary faculty for perception and the basis of legal obligation; it is known directly (badihi) and not derived from inference. Next, the author defines rational consideration (nazar 'aqli) in this research context as reflection on the sciences of the Law, especially concerning ijtihad. For usul al-fiqh specifically, nazar 'aqli means intellectual analysis of evidence to derive principles, verify them, defend against objections, and delineate their boundaries.

Key Terms
العقل (al-'aql): The tool of distinction and perception; a necessary faculty known directly without inference. [Note: Al-Sam'ani's definition emphasizes necessity and directness; later theologians may emphasize its acquired aspect.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Sam'ani as quoted in the passage, why cannot 'aql be known through inference?
A) Because it is a tool of inference and cannot prove itself.
B) Because inference requires knowledge and a basis, so 'aql must be self-evident.
C) Because 'aql is a necessary quality of all humans and needs no proof.
D) Because the Law presumes its existence without proof.

Key Takeaway
'Aql is a necessary, self-evident faculty that serves as the foundation for all rational inquiry and legal obligation.

?

Defining Nazar 'Aqli: Rational Reflection in the Law
Author defines nazar 'aqli as a tool for jadal usuli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the author's definition of nazar 'aqli and enumerate the four tasks it performs in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Nazar 'aqli is the active use of intellect to derive, verify, and defend legal principles.

Having established the nature of intellect, the author now defines 'rational consideration' (nazar 'aqli) as the intentional application of the intellect to the sciences of the Law, particularly usul al-fiqh. He specifies that in usul, nazar involves intellectual investigation of evidence to extract principles, verify them, repel objections, and delineate boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of nazar 'aqli
Find the section after the definition of 'aql, starting with 'ج - النظر العقلي'. Identify the author's explicit statement: 'the rational consideration we intend in this research is: a person's reflection and thinking with his intellect in the sciences of the Law.'
2
Identify the scope: sciences of the Law
Note that nazar 'aqli is specifically directed at 'the sciences of the Law' (علوم الشرع), not any random topic. It is a disciplined reflection within the framework of Sharia.
This distinguishes it from purely philosophical nazar.
3
Extract the first specification for ijtihad
The author mentions examining the conditions, rules, means, methods, and results of ijtihad. This shows that nazar 'aqli is the intellectual work of a mujtahid.
4
Extract the second specification for usul al-fiqh
For usul, nazar 'aqli is defined as intellectual investigation (bahth fikri) and rational reflection (ta'ammul 'aqli) on the evidence (adilla) to: (1) extract usul rules, (2) verify them, (3) repel any refutations, and (4) clarify their limits and scope.
This four-fold task is the core activity of the usuli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي نعنيه بالعقل في هذا المقام : أداة التمييز والإدراك التي يتمكن بها الإنسان من تمييز المعلومات بعضها عن بعض وتوليد بعضها من بعض، وهنا يمكن أن نورد تعريف السمعاني للعقل، حيث يعرفه بأنه: «قوة ضرورية بوجودها درك الأشياء، ويتوجه تكليف الشرع، وهو ما يعرفه كل إنسان من نفسه، ولا يستدل عليه بغيره لأن الاستدلال مفتقر إلى علم ينظر فيه وأصل يعتمد عليه، ولو كان غيره دليلا عليه لكان مكتسبا لا ضروريًا» (1) . ج - النظر العقلي : بعد هذا يمكن أن نقول بأن النظر العقلي الذي نريده في هذا البحث هو : تأمل الإنسان وتفكره بعقله في علوم الشرع، من جهة النظر في شروط الاجتهاد وقواعده وسبل الاجتهاد ومسالكه ونتائجه» . أما النظر العقلي في أصول الفقه، فنعني به البحث الفكري والتأمل العقلي الأدلة لاستخلاص القواعد الأصولية، وتحقيقها، ودفع ما قد ينقضها، وبيان حدودها، ومجال عمل كل منها .
What we mean by intellect in this context: the tool of distinction and perception by which a person can distinguish pieces of information from each other and generate some from others. Here we can mention al-Sam'ani's definition of intellect, as he defines it: 'A necessary faculty by which things are perceived, and by which the obligation of the Law is directed, and which every person knows from himself, and is not inferred from something else, because inference depends on knowledge to be examined and a basis to rely on, and if something else were a proof for it, it would be acquired, not necessary.' (1) C. Rational Consideration: After this, we can say that the rational consideration we intend in this research is: a person's reflection and thinking with his intellect in the sciences of the Law, from the perspective of examining the conditions of ijtihad, its rules, means, methods, and results. As for rational consideration in usul al-fiqh, we mean by it intellectual investigation and rational reflection on the evidence to extract usul rules, verify them, repel anything that may refute them, clarify their limits, and the scope of each.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms: 'aql (intellect) and nazar 'aqli (rational consideration). The author first presents a working definition of intellect as a tool for distinguishing and generating knowledge, then quotes al-Sam'ani's more precise definition: intellect is a necessary faculty for perception and the basis of legal obligation; it is known directly (badihi) and not derived from inference. Next, the author defines rational consideration (nazar 'aqli) in this research context as reflection on the sciences of the Law, especially concerning ijtihad. For usul al-fiqh specifically, nazar 'aqli means intellectual analysis of evidence to derive principles, verify them, defend against objections, and delineate their boundaries.

Key Terms
النظر العقلي (al-nazar al-'aqli): Reflection and thinking with intellect in the sciences of the Law; intellectual investigation of evidence in usul al-fiqh to extract, verify, defend, and clarify principles. [Note: Some later texts distinguish nazar as a type of qiyas; here it is general rational reflection.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Extracting usul rules from evidence
B) Verifying the extracted rules
C) Providing historical context for rulings
D) Repelling refutations that may invalidate the rules

Key Takeaway
Nazar 'aqli is the methodical application of intellect to legal evidence to establish and defend usul principles.

The Role of Nazar in Jadal
Author defines jadal and its rootedness in nazar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why jadal is called munazara and how nazar functions as the core of dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Jadal is not combat; it is mutual nazar to test evidence.

In this passage, the author clarifies that jadal (dialectical argumentation) is fundamentally an exchange of rational reflection (nazar) between two parties. Understanding this connection is crucial for appreciating the disciplined, evidence-based nature of Islamic dialectics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of jadal
Locate how the author defines jadal: it involves raising objections against the opponent's evidence, straining the mind to find weaknesses, and aiming to demolish their reasoning.
Notice the active mental effort required.
2
Connect jadal to munazara
Find the statement that jadal is often called munazara because it is an exchange of nazar between the two parties. This shows the two terms are nearly synonymous in this context.
Al-Shirazi's quote reinforces this: the assemblies of nazar are really assemblies of jadal.
3
Extract the purpose of nazar in jadal
The author implies that nazar is the method by which truth is distinguished from falsehood in dialectical exchanges. Jadal without nazar would be mere assertion.
This aligns with the later argument that nazar leads to knowledge.
4
Apply to an usuli debate
Consider a debate between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i on qiyas. Explain how they would use nazar to examine each other's evidence rather than simply citing authorities.
This illustrates the practical role of nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل اعتراضاته على أدلة المخالف، إذ يكد عقله ويعمل ذهنه في محاولة الوقوف على جوانب الضعف في أدلة المخالف ويحاول من خلال ذلك هدمها وإسقاط الاستدلال بها. لذلك كان الجدل يسمى في الكثير من الأحيان مناظرة، لأنه تبادل للنظر بين طرفي الجدل، قال الشيرازي: وسميت مجالس النظر بذلك، وإن كانت في الحقيقة مجالس الجدل، لأن الجدل الواقع فيها يقع عن الفكر والنظر. وفي بيان أهمية النظر العقلي ووجه الحاجة إليه في دراسة الشريعة بوجه عام، يقول الإمام جمال الدين القاسمي رحمه الله: النظر قانون الاستدلال في الأمور، وحاكم العدل، وقاضي الصدق، وبرهان الشريعة، ومحك الحق والباطل وبريد المعرفة وسلطان الحقيقة، وترجمان الإيمان، وحجة الأنبياء، ومحجة الأولياء، والسيف القاطع على الأعداء، شجرة طيبة أصلها ثابت وفرعها في السماء، فالنظر رأس السعادة عند أهل الدنيا والدين. فأساس التدبير وصحة الاعتقاد وخلاصة التوحيد في ناصية النظر، كما أن أساس الكفر والشرك في جانب التقليد. فإن قيل: ما الحجة على صحة النظر وأنه مؤد إلى العلم؟ فيقال: إن في العلم حقا وباطلا والناس صنفان: أهل الحق وأهل الباطل، ولا يتصور معرفة الحق من الباطل إلا بالنظر، والإنسان خلق كامل الرأي عظيم الفكر دراكا للمعاني، وأتي الإدراك وهو العقل، فإذا استعمله على وجهه وقع عنده العلم بالمنظور فيه، كما يقع العلم بالمدركات عند الإدراك، فعند فتح الأجفان يبصر الأشياء، وعند الاستماع والإصغاء يسمع، وعند استعمال اللسان يتكلم، فعند النظر يعلم ولو كان فاسدًا لم يتضمن العلم، لأن الفاسد لا يحكم له بقضية صحيحة. والدليل على أن النظر يوصل إلى العلم، وهو طريق الحقائق: فزع العقلاء إليه إذا التبس عليهم حكم شيء من الغائبات كما يفزعون إلى البصر والسمع في تعريف ما يخفى عليهم من أحوال المرئيات والمسموعات، فالنظر دليل العلم. ولما رأينا عقلاء العالم وجهابذة المعاني مهما نزلت بهم نازلة أو حدث لهم من المشكلات المهمات فزعوا إلى النظر وتفكروا وتدبروا ليعرفوا وجه الصواب من الخطأ والحق من الباطل، عرفنا بضرورة العقل أن النظر طريق العلم. أما في موضوعنا تحديدا، فيؤكد الإمام ابن عقيل أنه "لا يجب نصرة أصول الفقه على مذهب فقيه، بل الواجب النظر في الأدلة، فما أداه الدليل إليه، كان مذهبه بحسبه وبنى على ذلك الأصل، ونعوذ بالله من اعتقاد مذهب ثم طلب أصله أو طلب دليله، وما ذلك إلا بمثابة من مضى في طريق مظلم بغير ضياء، ثم طلب لذلك الطريق ضياء ينظر إن كان فيه بئر أو سبع أو ما شاكل ذلك، أو كان سليما". ومراد ابن عقيل رحمه الله: أنه لا يجوز لباحث أن يعتقد في ذهنه فكرة مسبقة ثم يحاول البحث لها عن أدلة، بل عليه أن يضع الأفكار كلها على صعيد واحد ثم ما كان منها قسي الحجة ناصع الدليل واضح البرهان، ولا يتم له ذلك إلا بالنظر والتأمل وتقليب وجوه الرأي والفكر. المطلب الثالث: شروط السلامة إن النظر العقلي حتى يؤدي وظيفته في الجدل الأصولي، ينبغي أن يتوفر الناظر
Dialectical argumentation (jadal): objections against the evidence of the opponent, as he strains his mind and exerts his intellect in attempting to identify weaknesses in the opponent's evidence and tries through that to demolish them and invalidate reasoning by them. Therefore, jadal was often called munazara (disputation), because it is an exchange of mutual consideration (nazar) between the two parties of jadal. Al-Shirazi said: 'And the assemblies of nazar were named thus, even though they are in reality assemblies of jadal, because the jadal occurring in them occurs through thought and nazar.' In explaining the importance of rational nazar and the need for it in studying the Sharia in general, Imam Jamal al-Din al-Qasimi (may God have mercy on him) says: 'Nazar is the canon of reasoning in matters, the judge of justice, the arbiter of truth, the proof of the Sharia, the touchstone of truth and falsehood, the messenger of knowledge, the sultan of reality, the interpreter of faith, the argument of the prophets, the path of the saints, and the cutting sword against enemies—a good tree whose root is firm and whose branch is in the sky. Thus nazar is the head of happiness for the people of the world and religion. The foundation of management, correctness of belief, and essence of tawhid are in the forelock of nazar, just as the foundation of disbelief and polytheism is in the side of blind imitation (taqlid).' If it is said: 'What is the proof of the validity of nazar and that it leads to knowledge?' It is said: 'In knowledge there is truth and falsehood, and people are two types: people of truth and people of falsehood. And it is inconceivable to know truth from falsehood except through nazar. And man was created perfect in opinion, great in thought, perceiving meanings, and he was given perception, which is intellect. If he uses it in its proper way, knowledge of the matter considered occurs to him, just as knowledge occurs of perceptibles through perception. Thus when opening the eyelids, one sees things; when listening and paying attention, one hears; when using the tongue, one speaks. Similarly, when engaging in nazar, one knows. And if it were corrupt, it would not involve knowledge, because the corrupt cannot be judged with a correct proposition.' And the evidence that nazar leads to knowledge and is the path to realities: rational people resort to it when the judgment on something unseen becomes confusing to them, just as they resort to sight and hearing for knowing what is hidden from them regarding the states of seen and heard things. Thus nazar is the guide to knowledge. And since we have seen the rational people of the world and the geniuses of meanings, whenever a calamity befalls them or they encounter important problematic issues, they resort to nazar, reflect, and ponder to know the side of correctness from error and truth from falsehood—we know by necessity of the intellect that nazar is the path to knowledge. As for our specific subject, Imam Ibn 'Aqil confirms: 'It is not obligatory to defend usul al-fiqh according to the madhhab of a faqih; rather, it is obligatory to engage in nazar of the evidence. Whatever the evidence leads to becomes his madhhab accordingly, and he builds upon that principle. We seek refuge in God from believing a madhhab and then seeking its basis or seeking its evidence. That is nothing but like one who proceeds in a dark path without light, then seeks light for that path to see if there is a well, a lion, or something similar, or if it is safe.' And Ibn 'Aqil (may God have mercy on him) meant: it is not permissible for a researcher to harbor a preconceived idea in his mind and then try to search for evidence for it. Rather, he must place all ideas on a single level, and then whichever of them is strong in argument, clear in proof, obvious in demonstration (and that cannot be achieved except through nazar, contemplation, and turning over aspects of opinion and thought). The third requirement: conditions of integrity. Indeed, rational nazar, in order to perform its function in usuli jadal, the nazir must possess...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a defense of rational reflection (nazar) as the essential method for attaining knowledge in dialectical argumentation (jadal), particularly within usul al-fiqh. The author begins by describing jadal as the activity of raising objections against an opponent's evidence, requiring mental effort to identify weaknesses. He notes that jadal is often called munazara because it involves mutual nazar between two parties. A quote from al-Shirazi reinforces that the assemblies of nazar are truly assemblies of jadal, as the dialectic occurs through thought and nazar. Then, the author turns to the importance of nazar in studying the Sharia, citing al-Qasimi's eloquent praise: nazar is the canon of reasoning, the judge, the proof, the touchstone, etc. Al-Qasimi emphasizes that nazar is the foundation of correct belief, while taqlid (blind imitation) is the basis of disbelief. Next, the author addresses a potential objection: what proof is there that nazar leads to knowledge? The response is that truth and falsehood exist in knowledge, and they can only be distinguished through nazar. Man is endowed with intellect and perception; if he uses nazar correctly, knowledge results—just as sight leads to seeing. If nazar were corrupt, it would not yield knowledge, because a corrupt thing cannot produce a correct judgment. The author then offers empirical evidence: rational people resort to nazar when perplexed about unseen matters, just as they use sense perception for visible things. Observing that wise people consistently turn to nazar in problematic situations proves by necessity of intellect that nazar is the path to knowledge. Finally, the passage focuses on the specific context of usul al-fiqh, quoting Ibn 'Aqil who warns against defending a madhhab without evidence. Instead, one must engage in nazar of the evidence and follow where it leads. Ibn 'Aqil compares blindly adhering to a madhhab to walking in darkness without light. The author explains that Ibn 'Aqil prohibits preconceived ideas; all ideas must be examined equally through nazar to determine which has sound evidence. The passage concludes by introducing the third requirement ('shurut al-salamah') that the nazir (one who engages in nazar) must possess certain conditions for nazar to function properly in usuli jadal, though the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms
نظر
جدل
مناظرة

B) Because it is an exchange of nazar between two parties
C) Because it is based on taqlid

Key Takeaway
Jadal is fundamentally an exchange of rational reflection (nazar), not mere verbal combat.

The Epistemic Justification of Nazar
Author provides proof that nazar leads to knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that nazar leads to knowledge and evaluate its supporting analogies and evidence.

Core Idea
Nazar is the path to knowledge, as inevitably as sight leads to seeing.

Having established the role of nazar in jadal, the author now provides a philosophical justification: nazar is the only way to distinguish truth from falsehood in knowledge, and its validity is proven by analogy and universal human practice. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim about nazar
Find the assertion that nazar is the path to knowledge and the only means to distinguish truth from falsehood. 'ولا يتصور معرفة الحق من الباطل إلا بالنظر'.
This is the thesis to be justified.
2
Examine the analogy with senses
The author compares using nazar to using sight, hearing, and speech: just as opening the eyelids causes seeing, using the intellect properly causes knowledge. This is an analogy of causality.
If nazar were corrupt, it could not produce knowledge, just as a broken eye cannot see properly.
3
Analyze the empirical evidence
The author appeals to universal practice: all rational people, when faced with difficult problems, resort to nazar (reflection). This is presented as an inductive proof that nazar works.
This is a sophisticated argument from consensus of rational minds.
4
Formulate the overall proof
Combine the steps: (1) Knowledge requires distinguishing truth from falsehood. (2) Only nazar can do that. (3) Using nazar inevitably produces knowledge (like senses). (4) All rational people rely on it. Therefore, nazar is valid and leads to knowledge.
Check the logical flow: is it deductive or inductive? It mixes both.

الأشياء، وعند الاستماع والإصغاء يسمع، وعند استعمال اللسان يتكلم، فعند النظر يعلم ولو كان فاسدًا لم يتضمن العلم، لأن الفاسد لا يحكم له بقضية صحيحة. والدليل على أن النظر يوصل إلى العلم، وهو طريق المعاني مهما نزلت بهم نازلة أو حدث لهم من المشكلات المهمات فزعوا إلى النظر وتفكروا وتدبروا ليعرفوا وجه الصواب من الخطأ والحق من الباطل، عرفنا بضرورة العقل أن النظر طريق العلم. أما في موضوعنا تحديدا، دليله، وما ذلك إلا بمثابة من مضى في طريق مظلم بغير ضياء، ثم طلب لذلك الطريق ضياء ينظر إن كان فيه بئر أو سبع أو ما شاكل ذلك، أو كان سليما". ومراد ابن عقيل رحمه الله: أنه لا يجوز لباحث أن يعتقد في ذهنه فكرة مسبقة ثم يحاول البحث لها عن أدلة، بل عليه أن يضع الأفكار كلها على صعيد واحد ثم ما كان منها قسي الحجة ناصع الدليل واضح البرهان، ولا يتم له ذلك إلا بالنظر والتأمل وتقليب وجوه الرأي والفكر. المطلب الثالث: شروط السلامة إن النظر العقلي حتى يؤدي وظيفته في الجدل الأصولي، ينبغي أن يتوفر الناظر
Dialectical argumentation (jadal): objections against the evidence of the opponent, as he strains his mind and exerts his intellect in attempting to identify weaknesses in the opponent's evidence and tries through that to demolish them and invalidate reasoning by them. Therefore, jadal was often called munazara (disputation), because it is an exchange of mutual consideration (nazar) between the two parties of jadal. Al-Shirazi said: 'And the assemblies of nazar were named thus, even though they are in reality assemblies of jadal, because the jadal occurring in them occurs through thought and nazar.' In explaining the importance of rational nazar and the need for it in studying the Sharia in general, Imam Jamal al-Din al-Qasimi (may God have mercy on him) says: 'Nazar is the canon of reasoning in matters, the judge of justice, the arbiter of truth, the proof of the Sharia, the touchstone of truth and falsehood, the messenger of knowledge, the sultan of reality, the interpreter of faith, the argument of the prophets, the path of the saints, and the cutting sword against enemies—a good tree whose root is firm and whose branch is in the sky. Thus nazar is the head of happiness for the people of the world and religion. The foundation of management, correctness of belief, and essence of tawhid are in the forelock of nazar, just as the foundation of disbelief and polytheism is in the side of blind imitation (taqlid).' If it is said: 'What is the proof of the validity of nazar and that it leads to knowledge?' It is said: 'In knowledge there is truth and falsehood, and people are two types: people of truth and people of falsehood. And it is inconceivable to know truth from falsehood except through nazar. And man was created perfect in opinion, great in thought, perceiving meanings, and he was given perception, which is intellect. If he uses it in its proper way, knowledge of the matter considered occurs to him, just as knowledge occurs of perceptibles through perception. Thus when opening the eyelids, one sees things; when listening and paying attention, one hears; when using the tongue, one speaks. Similarly, when engaging in nazar, one knows. And if it were corrupt, it would not involve knowledge, because the corrupt cannot be judged with a correct proposition.' And the evidence that nazar leads to knowledge and is the path to realities: rational people resort to it when the judgment on something unseen becomes confusing to them, just as they resort to sight and hearing for knowing what is hidden from them regarding the states of seen and heard things. Thus nazar is the guide to knowledge. And since we have seen the rational people of the world and the geniuses of meanings, whenever a calamity befalls them or they encounter important problematic issues, they resort to nazar, reflect, and ponder to know the side of correctness from error and truth from falsehood—we know by necessity of the intellect that nazar is the path to knowledge. As for our specific subject, Imam Ibn 'Aqil confirms: 'It is not obligatory to defend usul al-fiqh according to the madhhab of a faqih; rather, it is obligatory to engage in nazar of the evidence. Whatever the evidence leads to becomes his madhhab accordingly, and he builds upon that principle. We seek refuge in God from believing a madhhab and then seeking its basis or seeking its evidence. That is nothing but like one who proceeds in a dark path without light, then seeks light for that path to see if there is a well, a lion, or something similar, or if it is safe.' And Ibn 'Aqil (may God have mercy on him) meant: it is not permissible for a researcher to harbor a preconceived idea in his mind and then try to search for evidence for it. Rather, he must place all ideas on a single level, and then whichever of them is strong in argument, clear in proof, obvious in demonstration (and that cannot be achieved except through nazar, contemplation, and turning over aspects of opinion and thought). The third requirement: conditions of integrity. Indeed, rational nazar, in order to perform its function in usuli jadal, the nazir must possess...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a defense of rational reflection (nazar) as the essential method for attaining knowledge in dialectical argumentation (jadal), particularly within usul al-fiqh. The author begins by describing jadal as the activity of raising objections against an opponent's evidence, requiring mental effort to identify weaknesses. He notes that jadal is often called munazara because it involves mutual nazar between two parties. A quote from al-Shirazi reinforces that the assemblies of nazar are truly assemblies of jadal, as the dialectic occurs through thought and nazar. Then, the author turns to the importance of nazar in studying the Sharia, citing al-Qasimi's eloquent praise: nazar is the canon of reasoning, the judge, the proof, the touchstone, etc. Al-Qasimi emphasizes that nazar is the foundation of correct belief, while taqlid (blind imitation) is the basis of disbelief. Next, the author addresses a potential objection: what proof is there that nazar leads to knowledge? The response is that truth and falsehood exist in knowledge, and they can only be distinguished through nazar. Man is endowed with intellect and perception; if he uses nazar correctly, knowledge results—just as sight leads to seeing. If nazar were corrupt, it would not yield knowledge, because a corrupt thing cannot produce a correct judgment. The author then offers empirical evidence: rational people resort to nazar when perplexed about unseen matters, just as they use sense perception for visible things. Observing that wise people consistently turn to nazar in problematic situations proves by necessity of intellect that nazar is the path to knowledge. Finally, the passage focuses on the specific context of usul al-fiqh, quoting Ibn 'Aqil who warns against defending a madhhab without evidence. Instead, one must engage in nazar of the evidence and follow where it leads. Ibn 'Aqil compares blindly adhering to a madhhab to walking in darkness without light. The author explains that Ibn 'Aqil prohibits preconceived ideas; all ideas must be examined equally through nazar to determine which has sound evidence. The passage concludes by introducing the third requirement ('shurut al-salamah') that the nazir (one who engages in nazar) must possess certain conditions for nazar to function properly in usuli jadal, though the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms
نظر
علم
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main piece of empirical evidence the author gives that nazar leads to knowledge?
A) That nazar is mentioned in the Quran
B) That all rational people turn to nazar when facing problems
C) That nazar is faster than sensory perception
D) That only scholars use nazar

Key Takeaway
Nazar is epistemologically justified by both a priori analogy (intellect leads to knowledge like senses lead to perception) and universal practice of rational thinkers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the famous kalam argument: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated requires a cause; therefore the world requires a cause.' Explain how nazar operates in each premise and why, according to this lesson, the conclusion is knowledge.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Nazar vs. Taqlid in Usul al-Fiqh
Ibn 'Aqil's warning against taqlid in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn 'Aqil's argument that usul al-fiqh must be based on evidence (nazar) rather than blind adherence to a madhhab (taqlid).

Core Idea
Do not follow a madhhab; follow the evidence via nazar.

The passage applies the general defense of nazar to the specific field of usul al-fiqh. Ibn 'Aqil warns against the common error of first adopting a madhhab and then looking for evidence to support it. Instead, one must engage in nazar of the evidence and let that determine one's position. This lesson unpacks that methodological principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn 'Aqil's ruling
Find his statement: 'It is not obligatory to defend usul al-fiqh according to the madhhab of a faqih; rather, it is obligatory to engage in nazar of the evidence.' This is a normative claim about method.
He explicitly prohibits defending a predetermined madhhab.
2
Understand the analogy of the dark path
Ibn 'Aqil compares following a madhhab blindly to walking in darkness without light. Seeking evidence afterwards is like looking for a light to check for dangers—backwards and dangerous.
The correct order is: first bring light (nazar), then walk.
3
Extract the correct methodology
The author explains that one must place all ideas on a single level, then use nazar to test which is strongest in argument, clearest in proof, and most obvious in demonstration.
This ensures objectivity and avoids bias.
4
Apply to a contemporary usuli issue
Consider the debate on whether digital currencies are ribawi. A follower of a madhhab might assume its ruling, but the correct method is to examine the evidence through nazar without preconception, as Ibn 'Aqil recommends.
This demonstrates the principle's ongoing relevance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل اعتراضاته على أدلة المخالف، إذ يكد عقله ويعمل ذهنه في محاولة الوقوف على جوانب الضعف في أدلة المخالف ويحاول من خلال ذلك هدمها وإسقاط الاستدلال بها. لذلك كان الجدل يسمى في الكثير من الأحيان مناظرة، لأنه تبادل للنظر بين طرفي الجدل، قال الشيرازي: وسميت مجالس النظر بذلك، وإن كانت في الحقيقة مجالس الجدل، لأن الجدل الواقع فيها يقع عن الفكر والنظر. وفي بيان أهمية النظر العقلي ووجه الحاجة إليه في دراسة الشريعة بوجه عام، يقول الإمام جمال الدين القاسمي رحمه الله: النظر قانون الاستدلال في الأمور، وحاكم العدل، وقاضي الصدق، وبرهان الشريعة، ومحك الحق والباطل وبريد المعرفة وسلطان الحقيقة، وترجمان الإيمان، وحجة الأنبياء، ومحجة الأولياء، والسيف القاطع على الأعداء، شجرة طيبة أصلها ثابت وفرعها في السماء، فالنظر رأس السعادة عند أهل الدنيا والدين. فأساس التدبير وصحة الاعتقاد وخلاصة التوحيد في ناصية النظر، كما أن أساس الكفر والشرك في جانب التقليد. فإن قيل: ما الحجة على صحة النظر وأنه مؤد إلى العلم؟ فيقال: إن في العلم حقا وباطلا والناس صنفان: أهل الحق وأهل الباطل، ولا يتصور معرفة الحق من الباطل إلا بالنظر، والإنسان خلق كامل الرأي عظيم الفكر دراكا للمعاني، وأتي الإدراك وهو العقل، فإذا استعمله على وجهه وقع عنده العلم بالمنظور فيه، كما يقع العلم بالمدركات عند الإدراك، فعند فتح الأجفان يبصر الأشياء، وعند الاستماع والإصغاء يسمع، وعند استعمال اللسان يتكلم، فعند النظر يعلم ولو كان فاسدًا لم يتضمن العلم، لأن الفاسد لا يحكم له بقضية صحيحة. والدليل على أن النظر يوصل إلى العلم، وهو طريق الحقائق: فزع العقلاء إليه إذا التبس عليهم حكم شيء من الغائبات كما يفزعون إلى البصر والسمع في تعريف ما يخفى عليهم من أحوال المرئيات والمسموعات، فالنظر دليل العلم. ولما رأينا عقلاء العالم وجهابذة المعاني مهما نزلت بهم نازلة أو حدث لهم من المشكلات المهمات فزعوا إلى النظر وتفكروا وتدبروا ليعرفوا وجه الصواب من الخطأ والحق من الباطل، عرفنا بضرورة العقل أن النظر طريق العلم. أما في موضوعنا تحديدا، فيؤكد الإمام ابن عقيل أنه "لا يجب نصرة أصول الفقه على مذهب فقيه، بل الواجب النظر في الأدلة، فما أداه الدليل إليه، كان مذهبه بحسبه وبنى على ذلك الأصل، ونعوذ بالله من اعتقاد مذهب ثم طلب أصله أو طلب دليله، وما ذلك إلا بمثابة من مضى في طريق مظلم بغير ضياء، ثم طلب لذلك الطريق ضياء ينظر إن كان فيه بئر أو سبع أو ما شاكل ذلك، أو كان سليما". ومراد ابن عقيل رحمه الله: أنه لا يجوز لباحث أن يعتقد في ذهنه فكرة مسبقة ثم يحاول البحث لها عن أدلة، بل عليه أن يضع الأفكار كلها على صعيد واحد ثم ما كان منها قسي الحجة ناصع الدليل واضح البرهان، ولا يتم له ذلك إلا بالنظر والتأمل وتقليب وجوه الرأي والفكر. المطلب What Is Mantiq?

The Outcome of Rational Reasoning: Knowledge and Theoretical Perception
Author defines outcome of rational reasoning as knowledge or theoretical perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two possible epistemic outcomes of rational reasoning and distinguish the definitions of nazar given by al-Tufi, al-Baqillani, and Ibn 'Aqil.

Core Idea
Valid reasoning yields knowledge or theoretical perception, either certain or probable.

In this passage, the author consolidates the definition of rational reasoning by focusing on its result. When nazar proceeds correctly, it produces a scientific ruling (hukm 'ilmi), which is identified as knowledge (ma'rifa) or theoretical perception (idrak nadhari). These outcomes can be either certain (yaqin) or preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann). The passage also illustrates how classical scholars like al-Tufi, al-Baqillani, and Ibn 'Aqil defined nazar in relation to its outcome, enriching our understanding of the discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the conditional statement at the beginning: 'Rational reasoning, when completed according to its required conditions... leads to the perception of a scientific ruling.' This sets the premise.
2
Extract the definitions
The author defines 'knowledge' as 'perception of a thing through reflection and contemplation of its effect' and 'theoretical perception' as 'perception acquired through meditation and reasoning in evidences, moving from known to unknown.' Write these in your own words.
3
Distinguish the two epistemic outcomes
Note that the result can be either certainty (yaqin) or preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann). Certainty is absolute conviction; preponderant opinion is a strong probability. Both are valid results of nazar.
In Usul al-Fiqh, this distinction affects whether a ruling is definitive (qat'i) or speculative (dhanni).
4
Compare the three scholars' definitions
Al-Tufi said we know by rational implication that rulings arise from evidence. Al-Baqillani defined nazar as thought seeking knowledge or opinion. Ibn 'Aqil defined it as meditation and reflection. How does each emphasize a different aspect of nazar?
Al-Baqillani's definition highlights the outcome (knowledge or opinion), while Ibn 'Aqil's focuses on the process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر العقلي إذا تم وفق شروطه المطلوبة وسار على المنهج الصحيح كان مؤديا إلى إدراك حكم علمي. كما قال الطوفي رحمه الله: نعلم بدلالة العقل أن الأحكام تصدر وتحصل عن الأدلة عند نظر المستدل فيها طالبا ليعرف الأحكام. فتكون نتيجة النظر العقلي هي المعرفة والإدراك النظري. ونعني بالمعرفة والعرفان: إدراك الشيء بتفكر وتدبر لأثره. كما نعني بالإدراك النظري: الإدراك المكتسب بالتأمل والنظر في الأدلة التي ينتقل الذهن فيها أو بواسطتها من المعلوم إلى المجهول، فهو إذن ما يحتاج في تحصيله إلى عملية من عمليات الاستدلال الفكري. وهذه المعرفة والإدراك النظريين إما أن يكونا على سبيل اليقين أو على سبيل غلبة الظن. وقد عرف الإمام القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني النظر بحسب ما يفضي إليه من قطع أو ظن فقال: النظر هو الفكر الذي يطلب به من قام به علمًا أو ظنّا، وهو مطرد في القاطع والظني. كما عرف ابن عقيل النظر بالنتيجة التي يؤول إليها فقال: هو التأمل والتفكر.
Rational reasoning, when completed according to its required conditions and proceeded upon the correct method, leads to the perception of a scientific ruling. As al-Tufi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'We know by indication of reason that rulings are issued and obtained from evidences when the reasoner reasons in them, seeking to know the rulings.' So the result of rational reasoning is knowledge (ma'rifa) and theoretical perception (idrak nadhari). We mean by knowledge and cognition: perception of a thing through reflection and contemplation of its effect. And we mean by theoretical perception: the perception acquired through meditation and reasoning in evidences, by which or through which the mind moves from the known to the unknown. It is therefore what requires, in its acquisition, a process of intellectual inference. This knowledge and theoretical perception are either in the manner of certainty (yaqin) or in the manner of preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann). Imam Abu Bakr al-Baqillani defined reasoning according to what it leads to, whether certainty or opinion, saying: 'Reasoning is the thought by which the one who undertakes it seeks knowledge or opinion, and it applies to the certain and the opinionative.' And Ibn 'Aqil defined reasoning by the result to which it leads, saying: 'It is meditation and reflection.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage explains the result of proper rational reasoning. It begins by stating that when rational reasoning fulfills its conditions and follows the correct method, it leads to the perception of a scientific ruling (hukm 'ilmi). It then quotes al-Tufi, who asserts that we know by rational indication that rulings are produced from evidences when the reasoner examines them seeking to know the rulings. The consequence is that the outcome of nazar is knowledge (ma'rifa) and theoretical perception (idrak nadhari). The author clarifies these terms: knowledge is perception of a thing through reflection and contemplation of its effect; theoretical perception is acquired through meditation and reasoning on evidences, whereby the mind transitions from the known to the unknown. Thus it requires a process of intellectual inference. This resulting knowledge or perception can be either certain (yaqin) or preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-zann). The passage then cites al-Baqillani's definition of nazar in terms of its outcome: it is thought seeking knowledge or opinion, applying equally to certain and opinionative results. Finally, Ibn 'Aqil defines nazar as meditation and reflection itself, focusing on the process that leads to the result. Overall, the passage establishes that valid reasoning produces epistemically qualified outcomes—either certainty or strong probable opinion—and that this is the purpose of logical inference.

Key Terms
النظر العقلي
المعرفة
الإدراك النظري
اليقين
غلبة الظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two possible outcomes of rational reasoning?
A) Certainty and doubt.
B) Knowledge and ignorance.
C) Certainty and preponderant opinion.
D) Theoretical and practical knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Rational reasoning, when valid, yields knowledge or theoretical perception, which can be either certain (yaqin) or probabilistically preponderant (ghalabat al-zann).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The evidence from primary sources is ambiguous, but after thorough analysis, he reaches a strong inclination. According to the passage, what type of result has he obtained? Justify your answer using the terms yaqin and ghalabat al-zann.

Definitions of al-Nazar and al-Iʿtibār
Ibn ʿAqīl and al-Zarkashī define al-nazar as the heart's reflection leading to knowledge or opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of the definitions of al-nazar and al-iʿtibār given by Ibn ʿAqīl and al-Zarkashī and distinguish them from mere perception.

Core Idea
Al-nazar is reflective thought leading to knowledge or opinion, not just any perception.

Before exploring how jurists reason, we must understand what 'reflection' (al-nazar) means. In this passage, two major uṣūlī scholars—Ibn ʿAqīl and al-Zarkashī—define the very instrument of ijtihād. Their definitions reveal that nazar is a purposeful cognitive act, not random thinking. This lesson will dissect those definitions so you can recognize when nazar is properly applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
Find the Arabic terms al-iʿtibār and al-nazar in the passage. Note that Ibn ʿAqīl defines the former, and al-Zarkashī defines the latter.
Al-iʿtibār is often synonymous with al-nazar but here is treated separately.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for al-iʿtibār
Ibn ʿAqīl says al-iʿtibār is 'thought of the heart' (fikrat al-qalb) — this is the genus. Its differentia is 'aimed at knowing truth from falsehood and distinguishing proof from doubt' and it leads to 'knowledge or preponderance of opinion'.
The definition includes both a quality (distinguishing truth) and an outcome (knowledge or zann).
3
Extract the genus and differentia for al-nazar
Al-Zarkashī defines al-nazar simply as 'thought that leads to knowledge or opinion'. The genus is 'thought' (al-fikr) and the differentia is 'leading to knowledge or opinion'.
Note that al-Zarkashī also defines by 'negating the object of consideration' (nafy al-manẓūr), a technical move discussed in uṣūl.
4
Compare the two definitions
Both definitions center on purposeful thought resulting in either certainty or probable opinion. Ibn ʿAqīl's definition of al-iʿtibār is more elaborate, including the function of discrimination, while al-Zarkashī's of al-nazar is more concise.
The convergence shows that nazar and iʿtibār are almost interchangeable, but some scholars later distinguished them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتبار بمعرفة الحق من الباطل، والفصل بين الحجة والشبهة، وهو فكرة القلب، وتأمله، ونظره المطلوب به علم هذه الأمور، وغلبة الظن لبعضها. كما عرفه الزركشي بنفي المنظور، فقال أيضًا: النظر في الاصطلاح، الفكر المؤدي إلى علم أو ظن.
And consideration (al-iʿtibār) is knowing truth from falsehood, and distinguishing between proof and doubt, and it is the thought of the heart, its reflection, and its consideration by which knowledge of these matters is sought, and preponderance of opinion for some of them. As al-Zarkashī defined it by negating the object of consideration, and also said: al-nazar in terminology is thought that leads to knowledge or opinion.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines two related terms: al-iʿtibār and al-nazar. It opens with Ibn ʿAqīl's definition of al-iʿtibār as the heart's reflection aimed at distinguishing truth from falsehood and proof from doubt, leading to knowledge or probable opinion. Then it quotes al-Zarkashī who defines al-nazar similarly as thought resulting in knowledge or opinion. The phrase 'negating the object of consideration' refers to a specific technical move in defining al-nazar by its opposite. Both definitions converge on the idea that nazar is the cognitive instrument for attaining either certainty (ʿilm) or preponderant opinion (ghalabat al-ẓann), which is essential for the jurist's ijtihād.

Key Terms

الاعتبار
غلبة الظن

Key Takeaway
The definitions of al-nazar and al-iʿtibār converge on the idea that reflective thought is the instrument for attaining certainty or preponderant opinion in Islamic jurisprudence.
